On Wed, 31 Jan 2007, Tim Wescott wrote:

> Has anyone had any experiences with this place?  Does anyone have any
> recommendations for an alternate?  Are there any laptops, or laptop
> manufacturers that you've had particularly good (or bad) experiences with?

Tim,

   Several years ago I bought my current notebook from Emperor Linux, and I
would do so again. They put on the distribution I wanted and we compromised
on the partitioning of the hard drive. Their support is excellent and they
have a range of brands and models from the very light (running StrongARM
processors) to desktop replacements that weigh about the same.

   Prior to that I had two Toshibas (a Libretto and a Portege) on which I
installed linux. While the Libretto was too small for me trying to
touch-type on the tiny keyboard, I still have the Portege which I'll use as
a communications device when I don't need a lot of computing power on the
road.

   We also have a 1999 vintage IBM ThinkPad 600E that has run a Debian
variant, Red Hat, and now Slackware. When I have the TFT screen's inverter
replaced, it will be back at work again -- without the external monitor.

Rich

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Thu Feb  1 00:27:21 2007
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Thu, 01 Feb 2007 00:27:21 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Netgear FVX 538...
Message-ID: <20070201002721.oh7zz0mt6ocw04s0@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

The way it has been set up is creating a nasty problem.  When it is used
to go to the Internet, private services aren't accessible.  When the
original system it is supposed to back up is used, that system doesn't
have the VPN capabilities that this fancy shmancy router does and since
I am developing the original system to a certain degree, reliability is
an issue.

So my natural thought to avoid the issue of grabbing the wrong network
line and putting it to the other system is to set things up differently
so that the choice of system can be made on the host side without
touching patch cables in the server room.  I want to be able to switch
back and forth between these systems in events where an outage is not  
necessarily the reason to do so.  These systems are supposed to be in
parallel and it is hoped that at least one of them will always work.
My headache is, dhcp can't be used by both systems over the same media  
simultaneously, can it?  Putting in a second physical network line and
manual switches host side is a prohibitively expensive solution.  I
want to put an end to people unplugging the mains like yesterday.
This is too error prone, especially with all the patch cables in
the server room being the same color.

What is DMZ and do I want to mess with that on the Netgear router?

What is done in the professional IT world when you want to run routers in
parallel and you want them both to be able to use dhcp to configure the
same hosts on the same media?  I want to be able to choose either  
system regardless of whether or not one of them has failed to some  
degree.

A brutal software approach is to write a replacement for dhcp that
runs on the original system complete with client side software so that a
person can decide whether or not they want to ignore dhcp from the  
Netgear router and favor information from the original system instead.  
  Is this
the way I have to go to get what I'm after?

From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Thu Feb  1 10:00:54 2007
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Thu, 1 Feb 2007 10:00:54 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Netgear FVX 538...
In-Reply-To: <20070201002721.oh7zz0mt6ocw04s0@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <20070201002721.oh7zz0mt6ocw04s0@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <ca5165530702011000k4559061o6bf39148f83d2897@mail.gmail.com>

On 2/1/07, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
...
> My headache is, dhcp can't be used by both systems over the same media
> simultaneously, can it?
Yes it can but you cannot have the router/firewalls providing the service.

Normally for redundancy it is setup quite differently than what most
people at home would have.

You have two routers doing something like VRRP where both of them have
share an IP address but also have a private IP address that is
normally out of band
for management.  DHCP and TFTP would be used by the routers to grab
configurations
for startup.
>
> What is DMZ and do I want to mess with that on the Netgear router?
This is an area or range of addresses that bypass the firewall rules such that
any internet traffic can get to these addresses.  The Netgear router
(and most others)
support this but it is easily exploited by bouncing traffic off one of
these DMZ addresses
to other boxes.  This is because these DMZ addresses are on the same network
address range as the rest of your network.
>
> What is done in the professional IT world when you want to run routers in
> parallel and you want them both to be able to use dhcp to configure the
> same hosts on the same media?  I want to be able to choose either
> system regardless of whether or not one of them has failed to some
> degree.
Answered above.

>
> A brutal software approach is to write a replacement for dhcp that
> runs on the original system complete with client side software so that a
> person can decide whether or not they want to ignore dhcp from the
> Netgear router and favor information from the original system instead.
>   Is this
> the way I have to go to get what I'm after?
Every problem is tough if the only tool you have is a hammer.

On Wed, 2007-01-31 at 19:41 -0800, Tim Wescott wrote:
> Its time to retire my tired old Dell laptop, and I'm planning on 
> replacing it with one running Linux.  If possible, I'd like to either 
> get one that either doesn't have an OS installed and put Linux on it, or 
> buy one that comes with Linux preloaded.

One other issue to consider - if you're interested in Virtualization - 
VT (Xen, KVM), watch the CPU. Only some "Core Duo", "Core 2 Duo", and
"Pacifica" CPUs support VT. 

And many laptop mfrs disable VT - as I just noted on the Xen list

Many laptop mfrs disable VT, even if the Intel CPU supports it (AMD
VT-capable systems can't have VT disabled). There's a long HP thread on
the topic (which is also covered in /.)
http://forums.itrc.hp.com/service/forums/bizsupport/questionanswer.do?threadId=1051601

There are also interesting Wikis on the topic
http://wiki.xensource.com/xenwiki/IntelVT
http://wiki.xensource.com/xenwiki/HVM_Compatible_Notebooks

Thanks,
Mike

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Thu Feb  1 13:23:05 2007

On 2/1/07, MJang <mike at mommabears.com> wrote:
> One other issue to consider - if you're interested in Virtualization -
> VT (Xen, KVM), watch the CPU. Only some "Core Duo", "Core 2 Duo", and
> "Pacifica" CPUs support VT.

Look at the CPU numbers to find which ones support VT:

http://www.intel.com/products/processor_number/index.htm

Many vendors disable VT by default, but allow you to enable it in the
BIOS.  Consider the default disable of VT to be an example of
disabling a service unless you need it, which follows with security
best practices.

Jason

Michael Rasmussen wrote:

> scare with the T21, but it was a false alarm.  Oh, shucks, now I have two
> notebooks.
> 
> Wil and others have commented on T series suitability.  Is that price good for you?

I've had a T21 and a T42.  Both were excellent.  At the time I was
running Debian potato on the T21, and Ubuntu Breezy on the T42.
Everything was supported nicely.  Both were paid for by employer.

Purchasing my own, I've had a T30 (P4), and now an HP NC6220.  The T30
was thick compared to other T's.  XVGA+ is a minimum requirement for my
laptops (1440x1050), which makes it a big more difficult to find used
laptops.

When I hit Ebay, I found the NC6220 was a better deal than the IBM T's
(T43's).  They are similar in profile, features and weight, with the HP
having a more standard keyboard layout (CTRL-ALT is normal vs. the IBM..
though the IBM does eliminate that windows keys).  The HP also has the
mouse-nub like an IBM, which I prefer over the touchpad.

Where a T43 used with SXGA+ was fetching $1000-1200 at the time (a year
ago), I've picked up two NC6220's for $700-900 (1GB RAM, 60GB HD, Intel
b/g wireless).

After wading through all the Vista promotional crap at the entrance to
CompUSA yesterday, I did see a T60 on display.  Looks like they are even
thinner and lighter.  It will be a while before they hit the used market. :)

Anyone running Dapper on Turion laptops?  I like AMD, and have been
keeping an eye on Turion gear.

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

Concerning ThinkPads, the engineers at work have had good luck running

on the Latitudes for some reason.

Installing Linux on *any* of these machines is usually much easier 
than installing Windows XP, which requires a ton of manufacturer's 
drivers to work properly.

From ron at Opus1.COM  Thu Feb  1 23:10:41 2007
From: ron at Opus1.COM (Ronald Chmara)
Date: Thu, 01 Feb 2007 23:10:41 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] hard drive geometry...
In-Reply-To: <20070131000358.cmp371of408o4kwc@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <20070131000358.cmp371of408o4kwc@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <21722791-2AC4-41D1-B18F-3314FF19BFB3@opus1.com>


On Jan 31, 2007, at 12:03 AM, plug_0 at robinson-west.com wrote:
> Back around 1995 or so, we started to see geometry
> translating hard drives.
> .....
> Hard drive translation is extremely confusing to me.  I find
> it a very hard topic to understand.  Maybe I just lack a good
> explanation that I can follow.

Here's a metaphor for ya:
The current settings for distances between railroad tracks in the US  
don't make sense, by themselves.

Why didn't they ever increase the distance to handle bigger cars?

Because that could require a change to bigger cars, oh, and every  
car, for *many* years, would have to be able to run on both tracks,  
until the "wider track" was done rolling out, with new tunnels,  
changes to paths to handle heavier weights, etc. It would take about  
30-50 years or so.

With computers, there's a smaller timeframe, but what we are seeing  
right now is railroad cars/hard drives that can work in both  
situations. Mostly the legacy support is all about BIOS, and OS's  
(and the occasional rare application) that still live in a world of  
386 communication. If you want to get into the tech details, study up  
on BIOS and how most X86 computers boot.

> Do Apple computers have far less legacy support than PCs in order to
> keep them simple and theoretically produce a more stable platform?

That's a two part question, really....

First part: Tried putting a boot floppy disk into a new Mac lately? :)
Superdrive (floppy): Dead.
Boot from a floppy: Dead.
Boot from motherboard SCSI: Dead.
NuBus (card spec): Dead.
PDS on the LC series (card spec): Dead.
68K OS code support (Past CPU type): Dead.
Early PPC chip support (Past CPU type): Dead.
Localtalk/RS-422 support (Past hardware networking): Dead

Mac OS and hardware shed old standards every few years. Windows/ 
Intel, OTOH, still supports original IDE...<shudder> (Two hard drives  
on a bus should be enough for everybody?)

Second part: While wintel has kept up support and compatibility with  
programs (and viruses) from the early 80's, the mac platform (now  
mactel, they've shifted major CPU architectures 3 times now) has  
always had users who are willing to throw away older programs *and*  
hardware. This does eliminate historical problems, but it adds the  
problem of "how stable is a platform that *can't* run my program"?

In the process, apple has gotten *really* good at hardware porting,  
and at the same time, selling new boxes. Much to the chagrin of, oh,  
Mac users of older equipment, who don't want to change.
<http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timeline_of_Apple_Macintosh_models>

Is it more stable? I would say yes, in the same sense that buying a  
new car every 5 years leads to more reliable personal transportation,  
and that a 10 year old car may, or may not, be finicky.

-Bop

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Fri Feb  2 15:12:35 2007
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Fri, 2 Feb 2007 15:12:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] hard drive geometry...
In-Reply-To: <20070131000358.cmp371of408o4kwc@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <20070131000358.cmp371of408o4kwc@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0702021512x3e81cc28nf38992f8727964ed@mail.gmail.com>

On 1/31/07, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> Back around 1995 or so, we started to see geometry
> translating hard drives.
>

It is 2007.  The problem has been solved for quite a while.

> I'm wondering why the registers that the old CHS standard
> depended on weren't increased in size?  Where an 8 bit
> register was used, replace that with a 16 bit register,
> etcetera.
>

Because they couldn't.  The BIOS runs in 16-bit mode.  The registers
are only 16-bits wide.  For the INT 13 operation, the arguments are
packed into registers.  The cylinder (10 bits) and sector (6 bits) are
in one register.  And the head (8 bits) is in half of another.  The
reason for the 504 MiB limit with physical addressing is that IDE used
16/4/8 bites for C/H/S.  The combination means only 20-bits can be
used.

The solution was to translate from the logical addresses to physical
ones.  The physical geometry were pretty much fiction at that point.
The logical geometry was even more of a fiction.  Unfortunately, there
were multiple different schemes for the logical mapping and I remember
entering The limit there is the 24-bit space for CHS addresses.  That
was the 7.88 GiB barrier.

> I've received a comment that the register size can't be changed
> because: dos, Windows 95, and every other OS from that far back
> will break.  Someone also decided to throw in anti Linux fud by
> commenting that BSD is better because it isn't "cobbled" together
> with third party software, mostly from GNU, but I'm digressing.
>

The 1024 cylinder limit is why grub or other bootloaders needed a boot
partition at the beginning of the disk.  Once the kernel was loaded
and started running in 32-bit mode, it bypasses the BIOS and talks
directly to the disks.  ATA-2 introduced 28-bit logical block
addressing which can address up to 128 GiB.  Later ATA standard went
to 48-bit LBA.

> Doubling the size of the respective registers so that CHS will
> still work today with our larger hard drives seems to me to be
> a more straightforward solution.  I guess I don't see where the
> software problems lie.  Is it the size of on processor registers
> that are creating the: 16 heads, 1023 cylinders, 63 sectors
> limitation?  Are these registers in memory?
>

Since the existing operation couldn't be extended any further, they
had to add a new operation.  Instead of using the cylinder-head-sector
fiction, they went to logical block addressing.  I think the INT13
extensions support up to 64-bit sector addresses.  BIOSes and boot
loaders have supported LBA mode for a few years.

> Hard drive translation is extremely confusing to me.  I find
> it a very hard topic to understand.  Maybe I just lack a good
> explanation that I can follow.
>

You don't need to worry about it anymore.  I haven't had to manually
set the geometry of a drive in the BIOS in a long time.  My impression
is that all modern computers have the BIOS extensions and don't need
to worry about the 1024 cylinder limit for boot partitions.  I think
there is a 32-bit sector limit in the MBR partition table.  That 2 TiB
is a problem for some people.

The issue now is the replacement of BIOS and MBR.  The Intel Macs use
EFI as the firmware instead of BIOS.  They include a BIOS
compatibility layer so that Windows can boot.  EFI uses the GUID
Partition Table (GPT).  There is a legacy MBR which can by synced with
the GPT to have the same partitioning scheme.

 - Ian

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Sun Feb  4 10:29:19 2007
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Sun, 04 Feb 2007 10:29:19 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] dpkg for Fedora
In-Reply-To: <45C606B7.2010002@acm.org>
References: <45C605EF.8070608@acm.org>  <45C606B7.2010002@acm.org>
Message-ID: <1170613759.5145.4.camel@spartacus.nakedape.priv>

On Sun, 2007-02-04 at 08:15 -0800, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> Geez I hate replying to my own postings and especially this soon.
> 
> I see that apt is available for FC 6.  Anyone know if this break yum if 
> I install it?

No, it won't break yum, but it might not do what you expect.  Yum and
Apt are tools layered on top of dpkg and rpm; if building or
manipulating debs is what you want, you might instead try virtualization
and run an instance of Debian inside a VM.  Probably the easiest for you
to get going would be the free-beer VMware Server.  (You might even be
able to find a pre-built VM that's a Maemo development environment.)
The closer you can get to developing on the same platform the main
developers are on, the better off you'll be.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
http://nakedape.cc/wiki/

>>>>> "Larry" == Larry Brigman <larry.brigman at gmail.com> writes:

[I am moving this thread from plug-talk because the Meraki's run Linux.]

Mel> An article in NYTimes today about Meraki.

Mel> http://www.nytimes.com/2007/02/04/business/yourmoney/04digi.html?th&emc=th

Mel> Their website?

Mel> http://meraki.net/about/

Mel> I think it's interesting concept. I use to leave my wireless
Mel> router open, until too many knew about it. Once it started
Mel> hitting bandwidth limits, I had to take some security measures.

There are alternatives to cutting off your neighbors completely.  You
can do traffic shaping instead.  That lets you share without
completely slamming your connection.  Or just get more bandwidth.
Sharing is good and fun and orthogonal to security.

BTW, Personal Telco had one of the NetEquality guys talk at our
January Monthly Meeting last week.  We are hoping to get their
technical guy to talk at a future PTP meeting.

Larry> This is not too new.  I was looking at this back in 2001.
Larry> http://www.locustworld.com/ But then it was about $300-500 to
Larry> setup a working node.

The Meraki networks are based on a technology developed at MIT called
RoofNet.  As of a few years ago, it was running on relatively cheap
wireless access points called a Netgear WGT634U.  I've got a 5-node
neighborhood network running on those right now.  However, the WGTs
were EOL'd about 18 months ago and the supply has dried up.  Meraki
developed some new hardware specifically to support their needs, the
Meraki mini.  It has similar RAM and Flash specs to the WGT, and beta
versions became available last summer.  They are hoping to launch next
month.

NetEquality is focused on bridging the digital divide, but are too
small to help people get trained on using computers.  One of the ideas
floated at the PTP meeting was running some kind of clinic out in the
housing developments where these networks have been deployed to help
the residents work out their technical problems.  The FreeGeek model
(volunteer-at-FreeGeek-to-get-computer) didn't seem to be well-adapted
to this particular need.  PLUG members might be well-suited to help
out.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Sat Feb  3 19:27:14 2007
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Sat, 03 Feb 2007 19:27:14 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Postgresql on NFS root box...
Message-ID: <20070203192714.muiifw2xwggc4gog@web.robinson-west.com>

Well, I have postgresql-7.14.3 server running on Slackware 10.1
using an init script of all things that came with the source code,
modified of course for everything being stuffed under /opt.

Okay, what happens when the processor on this NFS root system loses
power?  The filesystem, an NFS volume, will most likely stay up.

So what are the cases for what might be happening when the NFS root
box loses power?  How do transactions work and what happens when they
are interrupted?  The filesystem the NFS root box is using should
still be alive.  On the NFS server, I want to be able to restore a
snapshot of the database prior to the last attempted change
automatically, if that is needed.

Should I periodically create a tar bzipped archive of the database
and in the boot process restore the last trusted image?  If so,
how should I go about this?  Bash daemon?

If I have to add a battery to the NFS root box, that kinda defeats
the whole point of not having a hard drive installed.  A battery
though may give me a chance to keep the NFS root box up for a few
minutes in an outage allowing proper shutdown of the database and
other services.  Dansguardian and apache seem to come back just fine
if I hit the reset button on the NFS root box, but I highly suspect
that postgres may not.

Is there a way for postgres to mirror it's changes to another
postgres box running the exact same database?

Runlevel 1 and shutdown/reboot commands need to be replaced
on an NFS root box because you will hang the machine if you down
the network interface.  Anyone know how to replace the standard
shutdown/reboot procedures and runlevel 1?

For those who are about to say google, how could I efficiently google
for relevant information about preparing for power outages on an NFS
root box?

      Michael C. Robinson

I am happy to see more schools use Linux in their Academic schedule. 
Especially to the point of employing different servers. I have taken the 
Linux Courses at Portland Community College and although excellent 
courses, I was disappointed that there was not much focus on server 
deployment. The classes do give a good basic knowledge of Linux and 
System Administration of Linux, but left most of the details of server 
configurations to finding them with Google Linux or posting on PLUG to 
find answers. Although a good lesson in how to find the information you 
need to locate there is still a lot of information left out concerning 
editing configuration files for servers.

Thanks
Randy

accessing the resource as long as it is all through one IP address.

Larry> If you are behind a NATing router/firewall, the ISP will have a
Larry> difficult time proving that you are sharing the resource.  They
Larry> don't have limits on the number of computers accessing the
Larry> resource as long as it is all through one IP address.

Furthermore, there are ISPs that are officially okay with you sharing
your connection.  There are four that come to mind, in no particular
order:

  Easystreet
  Stephouse Networks
  Integra Telecom
  Speakeasy

You might think that it runs counter to their interests (Comcast and
Verizon agree), but:

 a) they might not be able to police the sharing if they wanted to;

 b) having such policies actually drives business to them that might
    go elsewhere.

Not only are some of these ISPs supportive in their sharing-friendly
policies, but some of them (the local ones) go out of their way to be
even more supportive of the Personal Telco Project:

 * Easystreet donates the internet connection for our landmark node,
   at Pioneer Square;

 * Stephouse donates the internet connection for our neighborhood-wide
   Mississippi Avenue project;

 * Integra provides free colocation for one of our servers.

There may well be others that I am not fully aware of.  What goes
around, comes around.  These ISPs certainly have our warm feelings as
a result.

From tonyr at hevanet.com  Mon Feb  5 12:26:34 2007
From: tonyr at hevanet.com (tonyr)
Date: Mon, 05 Feb 2007 12:26:34 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] netinstall ubuntu on powerpc?
Message-ID: <45C792FA.3040409@hevanet.com>

I'm trying to figure out network installation for ubuntu.  I'm using Dapper
on a pc as the tftp server, and the target is an iMac G3/350.  The dhcp 
server
is a LinkSys router.  The installation media is a loop mounted 
alternate-powerpc
iso image of feisty/herd3.

I have gotten as far as having the first few steps of the installation 
dialog
succeed, but when it gets to the part about detecting the CD, the dialog 
fails,
because, of course, there is no CD in the drive.   I can skip that step, 
but then
the dialog fails to find the preseed file, and the process goes back 
into a loop
trying to detect the CD.

I'm not sure if this is one problem or two.  I'm pretty sure I have missed
something about specifying that there is no CD, but that the installation
source the tftp host, and I'm not sure whether solving this would affect
the yaboot.conf kernel boot parameter that specifies the preseed file
location.

What I've done so far has been cobbled together from various articles
in forums and wikis, mostly aimed at i386.  iMac netboot is started
successfully from OpenFirmware with "boot enet:<host-ip-address>,yaboot".

Any suggestions?


More detail:
1. tftp host uses xinetd in inetd_compat mode; tftp service is specified in
/etc/inted.conf

2. tftp root is /srv/tftp (tftpd installation provided that)

3. yaboot and yaboot.conf are in /srv/tftp on the host

4. the iso is loop mounted on /srv/tftp/cdrom on the host

5. So /srv/tftp looks like this:
   dr-xr-xr-x 11 root root      2048   2007-02-01    01:08 cdrom
   -r--r--r--       1 root root  153056  2006-07-25    07:02 yaboot
   -rw-r--r--     1 root root       4072 2007-02-05    11:58 yaboot.conf

6.  This yaboot.conf  got me this far.
## This yaboot.conf is for CD booting only, do not use as reference.
## Ubuntu PowerPC (feisty)

default=install


message=cdrom/install/boot.msg

# PowerPC subarch
image=cdrom/install/powerpc/vmlinux
        label=install
        alias=install-powerpc
        initrd=cdrom/install/powerpc/initrd.gz
        append="file=cdrom/preseed/ubuntu.seed quiet --"
        initrd-size=8192
        read-only

image=cdrom/install/powerpc/vmlinux
        label=expert
        alias=expert-powerpc
        initrd=cdrom/install/powerpc/initrd.gz
        append="file=cdrom/preseed/ubuntu.seed priority=low --"
        initrd-size=8192
        read-only

- tony

Wil Cooley wrote:
> On Sun, 2007-02-04 at 08:15 -0800, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
>> Geez I hate replying to my own postings and especially this soon.
>>
>> I see that apt is available for FC 6.  Anyone know if this break yum if 
>> I install it?
> 
> No, it won't break yum, but it might not do what you expect.  Yum and
> Apt are tools layered on top of dpkg and rpm; if building or
> manipulating debs is what you want, you might instead try virtualization
> and run an instance of Debian inside a VM.  Probably the easiest for you
> to get going would be the free-beer VMware Server.  (You might even be
> able to find a pre-built VM that's a Maemo development environment.)
> The closer you can get to developing on the same platform the main
> developers are on, the better off you'll be.

From jason at jasonjustman.com  Mon Feb  5 14:21:55 2007
From: jason at jasonjustman.com (jason justman)
Date: Mon, 05 Feb 2007 14:21:55 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] help with switches
In-Reply-To: <2e42bd9b0702051410t4143af36qe42831e97e43b9be@mail.gmail.com>

	<2e42bd9b0702051410t4143af36qe42831e97e43b9be@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <45C7AE03.3050408@jasonjustman.com>

you can usually do a mac flood and force the switch to dump the port 
into promiscuous mode - most switches have a 4k/8k mac address table.

http://www.rootsecure.net/content/downloads/pdf/arp_spoofing_intro.pdf

you're better off getting a cheap switch that can do l3 management and 
provide you with a monitor port/port mirror. 
j

From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon Feb  5 14:43:51 2007
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 5 Feb 2007 14:43:51 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] help with switches
In-Reply-To: <200702051400.35674.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200702051400.35674.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.0.82.0702051429040.1914@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Mon, 5 Feb 2007, Carla Schroder wrote:

> I'm having a hard time finding an answer to what seems to be a 
> simple question- what Ethernet switches include monitor ports for 
> sniffing all network traffic? I assume that unmanaged switches don't 
> have this, though it would be nice to be wrong because they're 
> inexpensive. I've been looking at all kinds of managed and 'smart' 
> switches, and I'm not seeing anything in their specs that sounds 
> like what I'm looking for. Is there some magic buzzword I should be 
> looking for? got any specific models to recommend?

In Cisco land, the magic buzzword is "span port" (SPAN == Switch Port 
ANalyzer; note that traffic passed to a span port has gone through IOS 
error checking, so it's not a "raw" stream of packets). 3com switches 
use "Monitor ports."

Other folks like Network Critical (www.criticaltap.com) market "TAP" 
products that sit in front of or behind a router. They essentially 
duplicate the live stream, one branch heads to the router, the other 
to the Intrusion Detection System (or the Bush Administration, if 
you're AT&T).

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

On Mon, 5 Feb 2007, Carla Schroder wrote:

> Thanks all, this helps. I found a Netgear switch for under $200 that 
> seems to do what I want, so I'll bet there are more in that price 
> range. NetGear ProSafe FS726T 10/100Mbps + 1000Mbps

My understanding (which may be flawed in many ways) is that Netgear's 
"port mirroring" functionality allows you to duplicate the traffic of 
*one* port, not a range of them. That's not a problem, of course, if 
all your uplink traffic passes through a single port.

I'll second Mike's ooh-ing and aah-ing over the features of such an 
inexpensive bit of equipment: not only VLANs, but also IEEE 8023ad 
link aggregation (mode 4 in the Linux kernel's bonding interface). 
Nice for redundancy and failover on a multi-homed machine in a 
multi-switch environment.

Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

except a broadcast loop on two ports will hang the whole switch even if 
its been properly vlan'd.  i had a vlan to another switch (alteon 180e) 
crash, and created a broadcast loop.  be sure to enable STP if you've 
got vlan's so that it can at least shutdown a loop..  but its completely 
the wrong way to handle this problem.

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Mon Feb  5 18:45:17 2007
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason Martin)
Date: Mon, 5 Feb 2007 18:45:17 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] help with switches
In-Reply-To: <alpine.OSX.0.82.0702051517560.1914@arrowhead.galois.com>
References: <200702051400.35674.carla@bratgrrl.com>
	<alpine.OSX.0.82.0702051429040.1914@arrowhead.galois.com>
	<200702051506.55869.carla@bratgrrl.com>
	<alpine.OSX.0.82.0702051517560.1914@arrowhead.galois.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840702051845j4121ed0br970d71cc69e80611@mail.gmail.com>

On 2/5/07, Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com> wrote:
> On Mon, 5 Feb 2007, Carla Schroder wrote:
>
> > Thanks all, this helps. I found a Netgear switch for under $200 that
> > seems to do what I want, so I'll bet there are more in that price
> > range. NetGear ProSafe FS726T 10/100Mbps + 1000Mbps
>
> My understanding (which may be flawed in many ways) is that Netgear's
> "port mirroring" functionality allows you to duplicate the traffic of
> *one* port, not a range of them. That's not a problem, of course, if
> all your uplink traffic passes through a single port.

Actually, unless you are monitoring a port running at a lower speed
than the monitor port (e.g. monitoring a 100Mbps port with a 1Gbps
monitor port), you will have a very good chance of dropping traffic if
you monitor more than one port.  Even a single port of the same speed
cannot be fully monitored if the link is saturated, since most modern
switches run at full duplex.  You would need special tap equipment to
truly monitor a full duplex port, by splitting each direction into two
ports that feed into a machine with two NICs.  You can then use
software such as the Linux channel bonding driver to combine each
direction back into a single stream you could feed to an IDS/IPS, etc.

If you don't do a setup like this, you're banking on the various links
being monitored never being saturated beyond the half-duplex send
buffer of the switch monitor port.  This isn't a great gamble, since
Ethernet traffic (and TCP/IP traffic in general) tends to be fairly
bursty.

Jason

Like a child at his birthday party I downloaded the 2.6.20 patch
yesterday and built me a new kernel.  This caused me to reboot my
machine for the first time since 2.6.19 was released, and it won't get
past Grub's stage 1.5.

I'm running Debian Testing on a 4-year-old IBM desktop.  Only one hard
drive.  I haven't messed with Grub or any other config since the last
reboot, but have run 'apt-get upgrade' every other day or so.

I booted off an Ubuntu Live CD (5.10, the latest I have).  'fdisk -l'
has nothing bad to say about my drive.  I mounted / and chrooted to it
then reinstalled Grub (on (hd0,0), hd0 being the only choice it offers
and hda1 being where I have /).  Grub claims success, but the same
error shows up on reboot.  I'm now trying to 'apt-get install lilo'
(still chrooted), but can't get it from the server.  I don't know if
that's because I'm chrooted from a live cd or just the occasional
unavailability I've been seeing from Debian's servers recently.

Can anyone suggest what's wrong here?  Googling for this error suggest
that it normally happens when the stage-1 MBR and later files are on
different HDs and one of 'em's missing.  That's not the case here.  As
far as I can tell all of the files are present and intact.  Clearly
part of the MBR is ok since stage-1 is running off of it.  I don't
know how to test the integrity of my entire MBR.  Grub at least thinks
it's ok when it installs.

Any suggestions how to proceed?

Thanks,

--Eric

On Feb 4, 2007, at 5:35 PM, Denis Heidtmann wrote:

> Russell Senior wrote:
>>>>>>> "Larry" == Larry Brigman <larry.brigman at gmail.com> writes:
>> [I am moving this thread from plug-talk because the Meraki's run  
>> Linux.]
>> Mel> An article in NYTimes today about Meraki.
>> Mel> http://www.nytimes.com/2007/02/04/business/yourmoney/ 
>> 04digi.html?th&emc=th
>> Mel> Their website?
>> Mel> http://meraki.net/about/
> ...snip...
>
> Russell,
>
> This brings into focus a question I have had for some time.  Won't  
> the ISP's financial interests urge them to seek ways to limit multi- 
> family access?  My ISP gets $18/mo from me and from each of my  
> neighbors.  It is easy to see that $18/house goes away with these  
> schemes.
>
> This must have come up already.  Can you point to some discussions?

The analysis of the costs and benefits for the ISP are a little more  
complex.  Here's a couple issues:

1) ISPs charge for bandwidth.  There's only so many of your neighbors  
that are going to be able to use the connection before you want to  
pay for more bandwidth

2) Fewer subscribers means less equipment, less provisioning, less  
support, less power; lots of costs that go down for them.


-kevin

Eric,

   You write that you downloaded the patch for .20. Did you use it to patch
the .19 source? If not, you can't build a complete kernel from the patch
file.

> ... but have run 'apt-get upgrade' every other day or so.

   Why?

> Can anyone suggest what's wrong here?

   Two things. First, try building from the full kernel source instead of a
patched file. Second, while it's been a long time since I saw GRUB rather
than LILO, you should be able to boot into the kernel you used before and --
according to Carla's Most Excellent Book -- you can get a command line to
fix boot problems.

From cooper at cooper.stevenson.name  Tue Feb  6 13:18:15 2007
From: cooper at cooper.stevenson.name (Cooper Stevenson)
Date: Tue, 06 Feb 2007 13:18:15 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Looking For Work In Portland
In-Reply-To: <45C7D3EE.3090503@cooper.stevenson.name>
References: <45C7D3EE.3090503@cooper.stevenson.name>
Message-ID: <45C8F097.7020109@cooper.stevenson.name>

Hello,

I've recently been asked to share some examples of some scripting 
skills. I thought these might be of interest to you as well--especially 
my geosynchronized photo script.

The first, process_main.sh is one of my favorites. I wanted a system
that would permit me to automatically create geosynchronized satellite
maps with labels depicting the exact location of each photo was taken. 
You may view the script online here:

  http://cooper.stevenson.name/scripts/process_main.txt
 

All the user has to do is simply plug in the camera & GPS and save the
camera's images to a directory. The script will automatically download
the GPS track log, synchronize the data with the image header data, pull
down a sat. map from the Internet, and display the results!

I like to separate my code as much as I can; this makes reading the code
for myself and others much easier. I have included my script performing
the "gruntwork" main task, process_inf_data.rb, as an example.
Process_inf_data.rb, written in Ruby, also serves t estimate to my
talent for writing scripts in various languages. Here's the data 
processing script:

  http://cooper.stevenson.name/scripts/process_inf_data.txt

A picture is worth a thousand words, so here's the output of the image 
geosynchronization scripts here:

  http://cooper.stevenson.name/scripts/geo4589-map.jpg

The second group of scripts, address.ksh & filter_web.sed is are part of
a series of scripts I wrote to automatically filter information from the
online OLMIS database. Later in the series I export and run queries
against the results in an SQL database. These two scripts illustrate my
ability to perform complex data extraction through automation in both
shell and Sed (yes, the Perl guys and girls will "crush" me for using 
Sed). Here they are:

  http://cooper.stevenson.name/scripts/address.txt

 http://cooper.stevenson.name/scripts/filter_web.txt
 
 
The last scripting sample in the series is rather complex; the script
reads exported GIS information and creates a hexagon-based configuration
file. I have included the script, "roads.sh," and the input GIS file,
"roads.txt." Again, this illustrates my ability to extract meaningful
information from complex input streams.

  http://cooper.stevenson.name/scripts/roads.txt

Not trivial work--here's the GIS ASCII input file:

  http://cooper.stevenson.name/scripts/roads_input.txt
 
I have dozens more scripts for things ranging from server monitoring to
complex Ruby on Rails based online GIS applications. I thank the PLUG 
list community for their patience in my posting to the main list; feel 
free to ask me for details or if you would like the source code.

Enjoy!


Best,


Cooper Stevenson
541.971.0344
http://cooper.stevenson.name

In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0702061751330.19314@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0702061740520.19314@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0702061751330.19314@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <659b9ea30702061806m3ee8b8fbo2e93145e6a7147be@mail.gmail.com>

Rich,

Udev still uses /dev.  Read up on udev rules.   It is super simple.  Create
a rule like this in /etc/udev/rules.d/10-chris-dawson.rules

BUS=="usb", RUN+="/home/cdawson/bin/usb_load"

Or,

ID_FS_TYPE=="vfat", KERNEL=="sd?1", BUS=="usb",
RUN+="/home/cdawson/bin/usb_load"

Use a perl script for usb_load and Inside of it print the  %ENV hash.
You'll see a ton of stuff in there, mostly symlinks which you can use to
mount a drive to whatever you want.

Make sure you name the file "chris-dawson.rules"  I am not sure if you rule
like I do.

Chris

From tonyr at hevanet.com  Wed Feb  7 00:02:55 2007
From: tonyr at hevanet.com (tonyr)

Subject: [PLUG] Re: Grub error 21 on reboot
In-Reply-To: <20070207052549.GN30127@amethyst.spiritone.com>

	<20070207052549.GN30127@amethyst.spiritone.com>
Message-ID: <45C987AF.7040100@hevanet.com>

For future reference:

The critical part here is the "setup" command.  There are three 
possibilities:
1. "setup (hd0)" installs grub in the MBR of hd0 (or hda, if you prefer)
2. "setup (hd0,0)" installs grub in the boot sector of the first 
partition, which
is NOT the same as the MBR.
3. "setup" with no arguments installs grub in the target specified by the
latest "root" command, which in your case was (hd0,0).

So it looks like you installed grub in the boot sector of the first 
partition
instead of the MBR.  You can still use that grub, but you have to make
special arrangements to chain-load it in the appropriate stanza in menu.lst
or lilo.conf.

I use the command "grub-install /dev/hda", which installs grub in the MBR
of /dev/hda.  I use Ubuntu, so I assume that it is available in Debian.

All of this and more than you could ever wish to know about grub is in the
Grub Manual 
<http://www.gnu.org/software/grub/manual/html_node/index.html#Top> .

- tony

1. It's nice to start with comments at the top of script that include
who wrote it, when, a description, etc.  Interestingly, I wrote a
template at one time (that I seem to have forgotten about):

http://haus.nakedape.cc/svn/public/trunk/small-projects/dev-utils/script-template

Here's another script that lets you extract POD from comments of shell
scripts, so you can generate man pages:

http://haus.nakedape.cc/svn/public/trunk/small-projects/dev-utils/unhash2pod2man


2. Er, you do know that 'date' can format dates as you like them,
without resorting to sed/awk?

$ date | awk '{ print $2, $3, $6}' | sed 's/ /./g'
Feb.7.2007

$ date +%b.%-d.%Y
Feb.7.2007

Anyway, since it looks like you're using the date for directory/file
names, it seems to me to use ISO-formatting, so dates sort numerically
in order:

$ date -I
2007-02-07

(If you want portability with non-GNU date(1)s you can use the
appropriate format specifiers.)

3. You seem to switch between variables named with '_dir' and
'_directory'.  Any particular reason?

4. Comments are easier to read if a space immediately follows the hash
character.

5. You have a couple lines longer than 80-characters, one of which is
really long.  Please, think of the kittens.

6. I think it's a good practice when building pathnames in variables to
separate them with '/', even if you plan to always end base paths with
'/'--someone else using or modifying your script might not.  I.e.,

image_mount_dir=${base_directory}08.09.06/images/

vs

image_mount_dir=${base_directory}/08.09.06/images/

(Not to mention comp.unix.shell FAQ about always quoting variables.)

7. You don't need to grep something if you're going to immediately pipe
it to awk; awk has a pattern-matching facility:

grep timediff | awk -F',' '{print $1, $2, $4, $5, $6}' 

awk -F, '/timediff/ {print $1, $2, $4, $5, $6}'

(The comma doesn't need to be quoted either.)

8. "run: command not found"  (You're missing a '#' at the beginning of
the penultimate line.)

9. "perl" not "pearl"

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
http://nakedape.cc

From pm at nellump.net  Wed Feb  7 10:07:03 2007
From: pm at nellump.net (Paul Mullen)
Date: Wed, 7 Feb 2007 10:07:03 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] looping over file names in numeric order in bash?
In-Reply-To: <45CA0EA4.4050405@seitzassoc.com>

On Wed, Feb 07, 2007 at 09:38:44AM -0800, Galen Seitz wrote:
> Given the following file names, is there an easy way to loop
> over them in numeric order?
> 
> data1.ps   data11.ps  data3.ps  data5.ps  data7.ps  data9.ps
> data10.ps  data2.ps   data4.ps  data6.ps  data8.ps

Use 'seq', which generates a sequence (shocking!). It's an
indispensable tool for shell script looping.

for i in $(seq 1 11); do
	echo data${i}.ps
done


-- 
Paul

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Feb  7 11:01:32 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 7 Feb 2007 11:01:32 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] looping over file names in numeric order in bash?
In-Reply-To: <45CA0EA4.4050405@seitzassoc.com>
References: <45CA0EA4.4050405@seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <20070207190132.GA25341@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Wed, Feb 07, 2007 at 09:38:44AM -0800, Galen Seitz wrote:
> Given the following file names, is there an easy way to loop
> over them in numeric order?
> 
> data1.ps   data11.ps  data3.ps  data5.ps  data7.ps  data9.ps
> data10.ps  data2.ps   data4.ps  data6.ps  data8.ps
> 
> 
> In other words, how can I modify this:
> 
> for i in data*.ps
> do 
>  echo $i
> done
> 
> 
> to give this:
> 
> data1.ps
> data2.ps
> data3.ps
> data4.ps
> data5.ps
> data6.ps
> data7.ps
> data8.ps
> data9.ps
> data10.ps
> data11.ps

---------------------------------------------------------

I suspect Randal can invent a Perl one liner that re-orders the glob.

It is better to generate file names with leading zeros in the number
to prevent this.  However, this comes up in version numbering a lot,
for example, the 2.6.12 kernel should have a higher value than 2.6.5 ,
so it is difficult to anticipate when zeros will be needed.

Some name sequences, like "2.6.12" "2.10.1" have even more tricky
ordering;  just stripping out the nonnumerals and sorting doesn't
cut it.

If the time order of the files is the same as the numeric order, you
might do an "ls -t data*.ps" to present the files in time order.  
That would do for a quick hack, but I would hate to depend on it.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From eehouse at eehouse.org  Wed Feb  7 11:36:02 2007
From: eehouse at eehouse.org (eehouse.org)
Date: Wed, 7 Feb 2007 11:36:02 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Grub error 21 on reboot
In-Reply-To: <20070207173927.0C37B4312@ulke.nakedape.cc>
References: <20070207173927.0C37B4312@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <20070207193602.GQ30127@amethyst.spiritone.com>

> > Date: Tue, 06 Feb 2007 10:34:37 -0800
> > From: tonyr <tonyr at hevanet.com>
> > Subject: Re: [PLUG] Grub error 21 on reboot
> > To: "General Linux/UNIX discussion and help;	civil and on-topic"
> > 	<plug at lists.pdxlinux.org>
> > Message-ID: <45C8CA3D.8000703 at hevanet.com>
> > Content-Type: text/plain; charset=us-ascii; format=flowed
> > 
> > When you say 'reinstalled grub', what exactly did you do?
> 
> * run 'grub' as root
> 
> * inside grub, run 'root (hd0,0)' letting it tab-complete to confirm
> that there's only on possible boot device
> 
> * inside grub, run 'setup'.  The resulting messages claim some non-fatal
> failures along the way but eventually success.

> For future reference:
> 
> The critical part here is the "setup" command.  There are three 
> possibilities:
> 1. "setup (hd0)" installs grub in the MBR of hd0 (or hda, if you prefer)
> 2. "setup (hd0,0)" installs grub in the boot sector of the first 
> partition, which
> is NOT the same as the MBR.
> 3. "setup" with no arguments installs grub in the target specified by the
> latest "root" command, which in your case was (hd0,0).
> 
> So it looks like you installed grub in the boot sector of the first 
> partition
> instead of the MBR.  You can still use that grub, but you have to make
> special arrangements to chain-load it in the appropriate stanza in menu.lst
> or lilo.conf.
> 
> I use the command "grub-install /dev/hda", which installs grub in the MBR
> of /dev/hda.  I use Ubuntu, so I assume that it is available in Debian.
> 
> All of this and more than you could ever wish to know about grub is in the
> Grub Manual 
> <http://www.gnu.org/software/grub/manual/html_node/index.html#Top> .
> 
> - tony

Thanks Tony.  That explains why reinstalling grub didn't work while
installing lilo did, and more importantly how to fix this if it
happens again.  I'll have to live without knowing why it stopped
booting in the first place. :-(

--Eric

On Wed, 7 Feb 2007, Galen Seitz wrote:

> One more question.   Why $(...) rather than `...` ?

See 'Command Substitution' section in a recent bash(1) man page. I 
think $(command) is the POSIX recommendation, though the "Shell 
Command Language" documents at opengroup.org list both as acceptable.

http://www.opengroup.org/onlinepubs/009695399/utilities/xcu_chap02.html#tag_02_06_03
http://www.opengroup.org/onlinepubs/007908799/xcu/chap2.html#tag_001_006_003

From sbeattie at suse.de  Wed Feb  7 12:13:30 2007
From: sbeattie at suse.de (Steve Beattie)

Subject: [PLUG] looping over file names in numeric order in bash?

On Wed, Feb 07, 2007 at 10:07:03AM -0800, Paul Mullen wrote:
> On Wed, Feb 07, 2007 at 09:38:44AM -0800, Galen Seitz wrote:
> > Given the following file names, is there an easy way to loop
> > over them in numeric order?
> > 
> > data1.ps   data11.ps  data3.ps  data5.ps  data7.ps  data9.ps
> > data10.ps  data2.ps   data4.ps  data6.ps  data8.ps
> 
> Use 'seq', which generates a sequence (shocking!). It's an
> indispensable tool for shell script looping.
> 
> for i in $(seq 1 11); do
> 	echo data${i}.ps
> done

While shelling out to seq is more portable, bash will also let you do:

  for ((i = 1; i <= 11; i++)); do
  	echo data${i}.ps
  done

Also, seq -w is handy when files have been named "properly" with leading
zeros.

>

From robert.citek at gmail.com  Wed Feb  7 16:03:14 2007
From: robert.citek at gmail.com (Robert Citek)
Date: Wed, 07 Feb 2007 18:03:14 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] looping over file names in numeric order in bash?
In-Reply-To: <1170871313.10070.35.camel@willow.odshp.com>
References: <45CA0EA4.4050405@seitzassoc.com>	<1170871008.10070.32.camel@willow.odshp.com>
	<1170871313.10070.35.camel@willow.odshp.com>
Message-ID: <45CA68C2.7080403@gmail.com>

Wil Cooley wrote:
> On Wed, 2007-02-07 at 17:56 +0000, Wil Cooley wrote:
>> On Wed, 2007-02-07 at 09:38 -0800, Galen Seitz wrote:
>>> Given the following file names, is there an easy way to loop
>>> over them in numeric order?
>>>
>>> data1.ps   data11.ps  data3.ps  data5.ps  data7.ps  data9.ps
>>> data10.ps  data2.ps   data4.ps  data6.ps  data8.ps
>>>
>>>
>>> In other words, how can I modify this:
>>>
>>> for i in data*.ps
>>> do 
>>>   echo $i
>>> done
>> Kinda hackish, but...
>>
>> for i in data?.ps data??.ps; do 
>>     echo $i
>> done
> 
> (I should note that the filenames being in any order in the first place
> is filesystem-dependent.  'echo' the wildcards and pipe through 'sort'
> for portability.)

Using sort will also take care of any missing values.  For example:

$ echo data{1,10,2,100,20,10}.ps |
  xargs -n1 |
  sort -k 1.5n

Since the original problem was to loop over files called data*.ps:

$ ls data*.ps |
  sort -k 1.5n |
  while read i ; do
    echo $i
  done

Or if one insists on a "for" loop:

$ for i in $(ls data*.ps | sort -k 1.5n ) ; do
    echo $i
  done

Regards,
- Robert



From galens at seitzassoc.com  Wed Feb  7 17:47:24 2007
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Wed, 07 Feb 2007 17:47:24 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] shell script to bundle postscript files into pdf with
	bookmarks
Message-ID: <45CA812C.4060004@seitzassoc.com>


Thanks the help today.  Here's what I was working on.  This
script processes data files, generates postscript plots,
and bundles them into a pdf with bookmarks.  I thought it
might be useful to someone as an example of generating a pdf
with bookmarks.  As you can see, I'm not trying to get hired
for my scripting skills, but it does work.

galen


#!/bin/sh

bookmarks=pdfmarks
output=all.pdf

cat > $bookmarks << 'EOF'
[ /Title (Document title)
  /Author (Author name)
  /Subject (Subject description)
  /Keywords (comma, separated, keywords)
  /ModDate (D:20061204092842)
  /Creator (hand written or application name)
  /DOCINFO pdfmark
EOF

count=0

for i in $(ls -v data*.txt)
do 
  count=`expr $count + 1`
  echo $i
  my_application < $i > input.dat
  gnuplot write_ps plot
  base=`basename $i .txt`
  mv output.ps ${base}.ps
  ps_files="$ps_files ${base}.ps"
  echo "[/Page $count /View [/XYZ 0 0 0] /Title ($base) /OUT pdfmark" >> $bookmarks
done

echo '[ /PageMode /UseOutlines /DOCVIEW pdfmark' >> $bookmarks

gs -q -dBATCH -dNOPAUSE -sDEVICE=pdfwrite -sOutputFile=$output \
	$ps_files $bookmarks



From gilmanhunt at comcast.net  Thu Feb  8 11:10:13 2007
From: gilmanhunt at comcast.net (Russ Gilman-Hunt (YAR))
Date: Thu, 8 Feb 2007 11:10:13 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] iptables vs sendmail access.db ?
Message-ID: <20070208111013.6cf9899c@badger.had1.or.comcast.net.>

if I use uceprotect.de's level 1 spam blocking list, it's set up as a
sendmail access list. I can alternatively parse it and set up iptables
rules with all of those ip addresses (though this is against
uceprotect.de's usage agreement)

If I treat making the computer rebuild the access database versus
making the computer parse the iptables rules as being 0, which would be
easier on the system, making sendmail check a 100,000 entry access.db
file or making the kernel check 100,000 iptables rules?

http://www.arghwebworks.com/

From ed at alcpress.com  Thu Feb  8 11:25:22 2007
From: ed at alcpress.com (Ed Sawicki)
Date: Thu, 08 Feb 2007 11:25:22 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] iptables vs sendmail access.db ?
In-Reply-To: <20070208111013.6cf9899c@badger.had1.or.comcast.net.>
References: <20070208111013.6cf9899c@badger.had1.or.comcast.net.>
Message-ID: <45CB7922.5050002@alcpress.com>

Russ (YAR) Gilman-Hunt wrote:
> if I use uceprotect.de's level 1 spam blocking list, it's set up as a
> sendmail access list. I can alternatively parse it and set up iptables
> rules with all of those ip addresses (though this is against
> uceprotect.de's usage agreement)
> 
> If I treat making the computer rebuild the access database versus
> making the computer parse the iptables rules as being 0, which would be
> easier on the system, making sendmail check a 100,000 entry access.db
> file or making the kernel check 100,000 iptables rules?

I've dealt with this issue several times recently.
I don't know what your definition of "easier on the
system" is, but I'd say that an indexed database is
much better than a linear search through 100,000
firewall rules.

Although, I did once have to use the firewall approach
with about 10,000 rules. I organized the rules in
chains of /8 networks. Thus, no one chain had more than
a few hundred rules. It worked well.

As for indexed databases, I recently ran benchmarks
on Berkeley DB versus Bernstein's CDB. CDB was about
twice as fast.

Another approach is to use your own private DNSBL.

Ed

YAR,

   Don't know if this information will be helpful, but my postfix MTA checks
these RBLs on each incoming message: zen.spamhaus.org, bl.spamcop.net,

them. I'm sure you can use the same approach in sendmail.

From: blitters at gmail.com (Steve D...)
Date: Thu, 8 Feb 2007 12:00:11 -0800

On 2/8/07, Dr. Terry L. Bonner <doctorbonner at comcast.net> wrote:
> I recently upgraded to Comcast Digital with Voice.  The new modem, a
> motorola SBV5220 seems to be a problem for interfacing with Ubuntu.  I
> finally got my desktop to interface, but when I swap the ethernet cable,
> recycle the modem and connect my laptop (an HP Pavilion ze4230 with
> Dapper, which had previously had no difficulty interfacing with the
> former modem) absolutely refuses to interface through DHCP.
>
> Does anyone have any experience or insights?

  I had a similar problem with a desktop system running Linux.  It
wouldn't pull a DHCP address when it was direct connect to the modem.

  Boot up your laptop and fire up Ethereal (or Wireshark, depending on
your distro).  Set it up to monitor Eth0.  Do a manual "ifdown" and
"ifup" on the interface.  You should be able to see the DHCP requests
going out on the interface.

  I was seeing DHCP responses on the wire.  My Linux box wasn't
recognizing the response packets for some reason.

> Any help would be much appreciated.

  I just installed a hardware firewall behind the cable modem.  I was
planning on putting one in anyway.  The hardware firewall worked fine
with the modem.  My system was able to pull a NAT address from the
firewall without any problems.

Steve D...

From plug at merlot.com  Thu Feb  8 11:56:04 2007
From: plug at merlot.com (Kurt Sussman)
Date: Thu, 8 Feb 2007 11:56:04 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] iptables vs sendmail access.db ?
In-Reply-To: <20070208111013.6cf9899c@badger.had1.or.comcast.net.>
References: <20070208111013.6cf9899c@badger.had1.or.comcast.net.>
Message-ID: <20070208195604.GC24973@merlot.com>

Russ Gilman-Hunt (gilmanhunt at comcast.net) typed this ...
> if I use uceprotect.de's level 1 spam blocking list, it's set up as a
> sendmail access list. I can alternatively parse it and set up iptables
> rules with all of those ip addresses (though this is against
> uceprotect.de's usage agreement)

I use the level 1 list, but it doesn't seem to be catching significantly
more spam that the level 2 list. I also see about the same number of
false positives. Just a data point; the level 2 list is about 10% as
large as the level 1 list.

> If I treat making the computer rebuild the access database versus
> making the computer parse the iptables rules as being 0, which would be
> easier on the system, making sendmail check a 100,000 entry access.db
> file or making the kernel check 100,000 iptables rules?

The db is going to be extremely fast, and iptables is a kernel module,
with any interrupt protection that may include (I freely admit ignorance
of how interrupts are handled when executing inside a kernel module). 

If iptables really walks a list of rules to find matches for each
packet, that would be far more expensive than a user-space db lookup.

Postfix only processes about 1100 emails per day on my server, and I see
no increase in load between no UCEprotect list and the level 1 list. 

--Kurt

From jeff at sharetechnw.com  Thu Feb  8 20:18:54 2007
From: jeff at sharetechnw.com (Jeff Moore)
Date: Thu, 8 Feb 2007 20:18:54 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Ubuntu And Comcast Digital Cable?
In-Reply-To: <20070208200007.1608F439A@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <28231786.1351170994734908.JavaMail.root@mail.sharetechnw.com>

>I recently upgraded to Comcast Digital with Voice.  The new modem, a 
>motorola SBV5220 seems to be a problem for interfacing with Ubuntu.  I 
>finally got my desktop to interface, but when I swap the ethernet cable, 
>recycle the modem and connect my laptop (an HP Pavilion ze4230 with 
>Dapper, which had previously had no difficulty interfacing with the 
>former modem) absolutely refuses to interface through DHCP.

 If you are directly connecting to the modem then you will most likely have issues with comcast. I am not sure what they do but they lock in the mac of the first host that connects and then deny access to other nic cards that connect(if they have different mac addresses). I am sure this is a measure to help them manage one IP per client. Try setting your Ubuntu mac address to match the other box you have connected(I think you can do it via ifconfig. Please correct me if I am wrong.). Another thing that worked for me was to power cycle the modem. Best solution is to get a firewall switch to front all traffic. 

Jeff M

purchase the "home networking" package, and then you can only use 5.
Personally I think it's dumb, really dumb.  But I'm just a peeon in the
corporate world.  Just buy a linksys router and your home free.   Check out
your user agreement.  your not supposed to use your service for anything
more than surfin the net.  Your not supposed to open up ssh tunnels or set
up servers unless you buy a commercial account.   I've never seen them
enforce this but I can tell you that they know exactly how much data your
sending and receiving.

-ryan

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Feb  9 09:18:12 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Fri, 9 Feb 2007 09:18:12 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu And Comcast Digital Cable?
In-Reply-To: <ff01ea180702081200m67cf595cw2ca3c296ee277110@mail.gmail.com>
References: <45CB7C5B.2060706@comcast.net>
	<ff01ea180702081200m67cf595cw2ca3c296ee277110@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20070209171811.GB17846@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Thu, Feb 08, 2007 at 12:00:11PM -0800, Steve D... wrote:
> On 2/8/07, Dr. Terry L. Bonner <doctorbonner at comcast.net> wrote:
> >I recently upgraded to Comcast Digital with Voice.  The new modem, a
> >motorola SBV5220 seems to be a problem for interfacing with Ubuntu.  I
> >finally got my desktop to interface, but when I swap the ethernet cable,
> >recycle the modem and connect my laptop (an HP Pavilion ze4230 with
> >Dapper, which had previously had no difficulty interfacing with the
> >former modem) absolutely refuses to interface through DHCP.
> >
> >Does anyone have any experience or insights?

Comcast looks for a specific MAC address for one machine on the
customer end.  The technician that installed it wanted to use a
Windoze machine to install, so I obliged with a cheap old laptop
running Win98 with a plugin PCMCIA ethernet card.  That card later
went into the Other Cheap Old Laptop that is acting as my firewall
router.  

If I was doing the same thing today, I would do it with virtual 
machines.  I would use VMWare Player (or other similarly capable
software) to run a Win2K guest and let them install with that,
then use a Linux guest instance (or the host) as the firewall.

One of the advantages of a PCMCIA card as the device with the MAC
address is that it is easy to move around, and ejecting it from the
laptop gives me one-second shutdown on the link if the Bad Guys
come a knockin' on my internet door. 

BTW, one trouble I have had with Comcast is that they occasionally
put me in NAT mode on a non-routable IP (like 192.168.x.x), rather
than assign a routable dynamic IP address.  Since I usually am either
surfing or talking down a VPN link to my offsite server, this is not
much of a restriction, but it can affect the setup of iptables and
other utilities that are cognizant of IP addresses.

Keith

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From jeme at brelin.net  Fri Feb  9 12:15:16 2007
From: jeme at brelin.net (Jeme A Brelin)
Date: Fri, 9 Feb 2007 12:15:16 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] interface porn

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0702091040010.8947@shaft.bitmine.net>


On Fri, 9 Feb 2007, Wil Cooley wrote:
> I wondered about standing up that long.

First, there's no reason you'd have to stand.  The thing can be made into 
a drafting table or even just a smaller screen on a desktop (search 
youtube for "multitouch" and see if you can find the drafting table 
video).

Second, standing is probably way better for you than sitting all day.  I 
know loads of people in the computer world who have switched to standing 
workstations and say they won't go back.  Sitting is a prominent cause in 
lower back problems.  I use a balans chair at home, which helps a little, 
but I think a standing station would probably be best.

> I can imagine that for certain environments, like a command and control 
> room or any place where monitoring is the main activity or where the 
> main activity is otherwise spacial it would be really great.

The graphic design and animation possibilities are amazing.  Also, given 
proper software, some of the mathematical modeling applications could be 
really good.  I particular liked the demo where they appeared to be 
attempting to verify that certain graphs were planar (and on spheres, no 
less!).  The GIS stuff is obvious, I suppose.

Lots of "main activities" could be made spatial, given the right kind of 
interface design.  I don't think it's too hard to see that many people 
have problems with this desktop metaphor and some of the other interface 
quirks that we've been stuck using for the past couple of decades.  A 
spatial interface could be more intuitive and easier to navigate for more 
people.

I'm excited by the idea, but don't really expect change because the ruling 
class (in this case, the geeks) are always too conservative and 
self-righteous to develop anything new.

As Neal Stephenson wrote in "In The Beginning Was The Command Line", if 
the automobile were a new invention today, it would have a trackball and 
pull-down menus for shifting gears.  It would be a nightmare, of course.

J.
-- 
    -----------------
      Jeme A Brelin
     jeme at brelin.net
    -----------------

From mikeraz at patch.com  Fri Feb  9 14:55:05 2007
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Fri, 9 Feb 2007 14:55:05 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] bind-named
In-Reply-To: <727fc2ee0702091326q232baa85t31d67bb794bb9db7@mail.gmail.com>
References: <727fc2ee0702091326q232baa85t31d67bb794bb9db7@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20070209225505.GA6166@patch.com>

User Iam wrote:
> Have problems with zone files
 
> root at www user-iam #>  named-checkzone revp.1.2.3  revp.1.2.3
> revp.1.2.3:6: ignoring out-of-zone data (john.biz.3.2.1.in-addr.arpa)
> zone revp.1.2.3/IN: has 0 SOA records
> zone revp.12.3/IN: has no NS records
> 
> 
> The file
> 
> 
> root at www user-iam #> less revp.1.2.3
> ; revp 1.2.3
> ;
> ;
> $TTL 1D
> 3.2.1.in-addr.arpa.    IN SOA  www.john.biz. root.john.biz. (

this line is not in a valid format an example of a valid one is:
@       IN      SOA     ns.patch.com. mikeraz.patch.com. (

3.2.1.in-addr.arpa. would be a 



> ; define the authoritative name server
>        NS      www.john.biz.

do you give www.john.biz. an address later on in the zone file or did we see
the whole thing?

To show a full, but small, example:

$TTL 14400
@       IN      SOA     ns.patch.com. mikeraz.patch.com. (
                        2007011201      ; serial, todays date + todays serial #
                        4H              ; refresh, seconds
                        2H              ; retry, seconds
                        1W              ; expire, seconds
                        8H )            ; minimum, seconds
;
                IN      NS      ns.patch.com.
                IN      MX      10 tire.patch.com.   ; Primary Mail Exchanger
$ORIGIN patch.com.
;
@               IN      A       66.93.39.34
ns              IN      A       66.93.39.87
tire            IN      A       66.93.39.86


You'll not my serial number is formatted a bit differently from yours.
Keep in mind that the serial is an integer that increases with each
change set to the DNS information.  0 padding allows use of date string + serial
while keeping a consistant length to the number.

 SCP(1)                    BSD General Commands Manual                   SCP(1)

 NAME
      scp - secure copy (remote file copy program)

 SYNOPSIS
      scp [-1246BCpqrv] [-c cipher] [-F ssh_config] [-i identity_file]
          [-l limit] [-o ssh_option] [-P port] [-S program]
          [[user@]host1:]file1 [...] [[user@]host2:]file2

 DESCRIPTION
      scp copies files between hosts on a network.  It uses ssh(1) for data
      transfer, and uses the same authentication and provides the same security
      as ssh(1).  Unlike rcp(1), scp will ask for passwords or passphrases if
      they are needed for authentication.

Yeah.  Look at the raw man file:

  $ zless /usr/share/man/man1/scp.1.gz

or substitute wherever yours lives:

  [...]
  .Sh NAME
  .Nm scp
  .Nd secure copy (remote file copy program)
  .Sh SYNOPSIS
  .Nm scp
  .Bk -words
  .Op Fl 1246BCpqrv
  .Op Fl c Ar cipher
  .Op Fl F Ar ssh_config
  .Op Fl i Ar identity_file
  .Op Fl l Ar limit
  .Op Fl o Ar ssh_option
  .Op Fl P Ar port
  .Op Fl S Ar program
  .Sm off
  .Oo
  .Op Ar user No @
  .Ar host1 No :
  .Oc Ns Ar file1
  .Sm on
  .Op Ar ...
  .Sm off
  [...]

It appears your man is misformatting.

Subject: [PLUG] bind-named
In-Reply-To: <727fc2ee0702091544w21b67caege117084f00149ad3@mail.gmail.com>

Message-ID: <20070210073333.GA3614@patch.com>

User Iam wrote:
> Ok..
> 
> Thanks for the reply...
> That is standard zone file..
> I was having problems with reverse..
 
Then where are your pointer records?  or to continue with samples:

For subnet 199.88.144.0/24 in named.conf you'd have:
   zone "144.88.199.in-addr.arpa" {
      type master;
      file "199.88.144.in-addr.arpa";
   };

file 199.88.144.in-addr.arpa might contain:
 
$TTL 36000
@       IN      SOA     tire.patch.com. mikeraz.patch.com. (
                        2005111301      ; serial, todays date + todays serial #
                        8H              ; refresh, seconds
                        2H              ; retry, seconds
                        1W              ; expire, seconds
                        1D )            ; minimum, seconds
;
                NS      tire.patch.com.      ; Inet Address of name server
;
1               PTR     fog.patch.com.
5               PTR     hp5.patch.com.
11              PTR     tire.patch.com.

anymore than what's below):

"...
 I *do* know
that there is something  wrong with the way moto4lin establishes
connections to the phone.  I've never been able to connect solely
through moto4lin.  IIRC, the proceedure I've used to transfer files
to/from the phone is:  (If this doesn't work, let me know.  I don't
have my usb cable to test and it's been a while.)

1) run p2ktest and establish a connection, then close p2ktest (the
closing part may be optional)
2) start moto4lin, verify the settings are correct (pointing to the
correct tty device, mostly -- and in agreement with anything learned
from p2ktest in #1)
3) Hit the Connect/Disconnect button
4) transfer files very carefully.  The defaults are anything but
intuitive, and the view of the phone filesystem seems to have little
effect on where things go and what paths you need to input manually.
Basically, moto4lin is a serious pain in the ass to use, but I have
been able to get the job done with it.
..."



>

> Dick Steffens

through the phone's internet service thingie.  One can get around this, 
however, by using mencoder to just write a video file in the phone's

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sat Feb 10 17:52:50 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sat, 10 Feb 2007 17:52:50 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Moto4Lin and Razr V3
In-Reply-To: <20070210170928.b87d0708.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <45CCFA00.1000808@comcast.net>
	<c834ec80702101543g1319b397r2d0e7dccfdc80339@mail.gmail.com>
	<20070210170928.b87d0708.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20070210175250.08975b17.johnxj@comcast.net>

Omigod, I just did it!

Didn't need no p2ktest, no p2kmoto, nuthin' at all. Just moto4lin and
the ability to read google.

OK, here's the secret:

Open /home/<username/.qt/moto4linrc. This is what mine says now, but
you can see how I changed the first line:

[device]
#cfgACMdevice=/dev/usb/acm/0
cfgACMdevice=/dev/ttyACM0
cfgATproduct=4902
cfgATvendor=22b8
cfgAutoConnect=0
cfgP2Kproduct=4901
cfgP2Kvendor=22b8

[filemanager]
cfgAutoExpandDirTree=0
cfgAutoUpdateFileList=0
cfgGoLastFolder=0
cfgLoadList=0

Save the file and relaunch moto4lin. Go into Preferences and set the
device to p2k instead of AT. Once the bottom window in moto4lin says
the phone is connected click on the Update List button to see the files
on the phone.

I don't know if it will work to upload files like ringtones, etc. All I
wanted to do was download pics, and that worked fine.

The stupid part is that you should be able to make these changes from
the Preferences window, but when you do the changes are not written to
the moto4linrc file. You have to edit it manually.

Followup:

Moto4lin did not install a launch item in the Applications list, so I
decided to do it myself manually. But when I launched it from my new
listing I could not connect to the phone. After much gnashing of teeth
I discovered that I have to launch it as root to see the phone. OK,
good ol' gksudo did the trick.

But then while fiddling with it suddenly moto4lin could not see the
phone. That is, it said it was connected, but the file browser windows
wouldn't display anything and all I could get was error messages in the
bottom window in moto4lin saying that the search could not be
completed. Eventually I decided to recheck the cable. I picked up the
phone and noticed that the display was black. I opened it up and the
inner display was completely black, like it was turned off. I tried
turning it on, but it was dead.

I was just about to scream curses at moto4lin for killing my phone when
suddenly it came back to life. (Whew!)

Everything seems to be working normally now, but if moto4lin kills your
phone, disconnect it and let it sit for a few minutes.


From johnxj at comcast.net  Sat Feb 10 19:53:52 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sat, 10 Feb 2007 19:53:52 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Device ID in Grub
Message-ID: <20070210195352.15180df9.johnxj@comcast.net>

I swear I am never going to upgrade a kernel again. That's it. Ubuntu
screws up Grub every time. This is the third time in a row and I've had
it!

So I upgraded the kernel to 2.6.17-11 the other day. Today I decided
I'd better reboot to make sure it was working. And sure enough, Grub
says "Error 15: File not found." Grrrr.

So, since I now know a lot about Grub I went back to the boot screen
and hit e for edit. I noticed that it was pointing to (hd0,2) when the
installation that was upgraded is on (hd0,1). (There is another
installation of Dapper on hda3, and Ubuntu thinks that is where it put
the new kernel when, in fact, it correctly put it in the hda2
installation. Lame.) OK, I fixed that error, but it still wouldn't
boot. After much futzing around I noticed that it had changed the
device ID line. It used to say:

kernel /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.17-10-generic root=/dev/hda2 ro quiet splash

The upgrade changed it to:

kernel /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.17-11-generic
root-UUID=big_long-ass_string_o'_numbers ro quiet splash

So I changed the big long-ass string of numbers back to /dev/hda2, and
happily was able to boot again. (These things scare the hell out of
me.) Uname -r says I am now at 2.6.17-11-generic.

Two questions that the extensive Grub documentation I have does not
address: 

1) Although I booted back to my usual installation, the boot screen
went through pages of stuff, including a lot of error messages. It was
too fast to read any of them, but there was a lot. It never used to do
that. 

2) More important, and possibly related to the first question,
apparently the long-ass string of numbers identifies someplace on the
hard disk where Grub is to find the boot folder. (I did copy it down
faithfully so I can put it back if necessary.) But why use something
that humans can't understand when /dev/hda2 works as well. Or does it
work as well?

Having just fixed ndiswrapper again (half an hour this time around), I
remember now why it keeps getting broken with kernel upgrades. The
problem is that I am using amd64 Ubuntu, and I was using ndiswrapper
1.34, the latest version. However, Synaptic lists only 1.18. That's
because 1.34 isn't in my repositories yet. To install 1.34 I had to use
make install. And I think that make-installs it just for your current
kernel. So I had to uninstall it and then reinstall it. I don't know if
a CONFIG_PROTECT variable would help here. If it would, I'm all ears.

Much more annoying is that every kernel upgrade hoses my Grub menu.lst
file. Rendering the computer unbootable is not a nice thing to do to a
user. It's because there is another installation of Ubuntu Dapper on
hda3. So I upgraded the installation on hda2, it installed the new kernel
files on hda2, and then it changed the Grub menu.lst file so that it
will look for those files on hda3. Real bright.


From ehem at m5p.com  Sat Feb 10 22:51:58 2007
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sat, 10 Feb 2007 22:51:58 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Device ID in Grub
In-Reply-To: <20070211061446.8E0A34265@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200702110651.l1B6pwk8054296@m5p.com>

>From: John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net>
> 1) Although I booted back to my usual installation, the boot screen
> went through pages of stuff, including a lot of error messages. It was
> too fast to read any of them, but there was a lot. It never used to do
> that. 

Run the program `dmesg`, that will retrieve those messages. You'll likely
want to pipe it through `more` or `less` in order to view them. Error
messages on startup are generally bad, don't ignore it when your system
screams.

> 2) More important, and possibly related to the first question,
> apparently the long-ass string of numbers identifies someplace on the
> hard disk where Grub is to find the boot folder. (I did copy it down
> faithfully so I can put it back if necessary.) But why use something
> that humans can't understand when /dev/hda2 works as well. Or does it
> work as well? 

The problem is that device identifiers can change. If a disk is added or
moved, everything can change. While the UUID is supposed to be completely
unique to the filesystem, therefore even if the device moves around you
can still uniquely identify the filesystem.

You can retrieve the UUID with `tune2fs -l` on the device, and ensure the
grub config has the right thing.


>From: Carlos Konstanski <ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com>
> I always wonder why people prefer debian knock-offs to the real McCoy.
> With straight-up debian you don't get kernel package management; you
> have to download them from www.kernel.org and build them yourself (if
> this has changed in the last 2 years since I've moved away from
> debian, then my apologies).

That method works with any distribution, but it isn't the Right Way(tm).
With Debian and derivatives the Right Way(tm) is to use the
"kernel-package" script to wrap the kernel build and produce a Debian
package you can install with `dpkg`. This keeps everything nice and tidy,
though a small number of kernels aren't difficult to handle by hand.

> Is there no mechanism in apt to protect config files of your choosing,
> like gentoo portage has?  In portage, you just add a CONFIG_PROTECT
> variable and value to /etc/make.conf.

John, hopefully you're still reading.

There are three control files for kernel-package. The first is
/etc/kernel-pkg.conf, which can be overridden by a .kernel-pkg.conf file
in your home directory. This controls build-time settings, such as what
config target to use. Then there is /etc/kernel-img.conf, this controls
behavior on installation of the packaged kernel. Such as whether the
bootloader installation program gets rerun, whether the boot loader
config is modified to boot the new kernel, etc.

John, look at the man page for kernel-img.conf to find out all the
wonderful settings you can play with (notably disable). Carlos, the main
program for kernel-package is `make-kpkg`. The key advantage is it gives
you a very nice way to /uninstall/ old kernels.
