Maybe you could help me out a little bit more.  From the article:

"This burden extends beyond DRM'd music into games as well. For example 
the content-protected version of the game Flatout 2 runs 15% slower than 
the same game without content protection)."

The article links to the content protection StarForce:
   http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/StarForce

The wiki and the article do not indicate DRM+Vista=bad gaming 
performance.  I interpret this as comparing DRM's overhead in Vista, 
where it applies, to that of content protection in video games, where 
used.  I don't see him saying that Vista is the culprit of your claims.

Additionally, I see several gaming sites that show benchmarks between XP 
and Vista with most games showing single digit differences in FPS.  This 
is what I was alluding too.  With better driver support for the new OS, 
these benchmarks will get closer.

 From http://www.cs.auckland.ac.nz/~pgut001/pubs/vista_cost.html

/snip/
As part of the bus-protection scheme, devices are required to 
implement AES-128 encryption in order to receive content from Vista. 
This has to be done via a hardware decryption engine on the graphics 
chip, which would typically be implemented by throwing away a GPU 
rendering pipeline or two to make room for the AES engine.
/snip/
Needless to say, the need to develop, test, and integrate encryption 
engines into audio/video devices will only add to their cost, as 
covered in 
<http://www.cs.auckland.ac.nz/%7Epgut001/pubs/vista_cost.html#hardware>Increased 
Hardware Costs above, and the fact that they're losing precious 
performance in order to accommodate Vista's content protection will 
make gamers less than happy.
/snip/

and thanks Michael, but I just follow the links offered here by you 
and others ;-)

Even on gentoo, you would have to actually inspect the code in all
those source tarballs.  It simply isn't feasible.  You just have to
trust.  The kernel would be a nightmare to audit.  Such low-level code
can have bugs that are virtually inpossible to detect, especially in
assembly routines.

There's always the "if you can't beat them, join them" philosophy.
The most paranoid OS I can think of is Red Flag Linux.  You could just
run that.  You'd want to make sure you're getting a real Chinese copy,
not something they've adulterated to infect the rest of the world
with.

Carlos Konstanski

Just thought I'd mention that VMPlayer will not help you to install a
new guest OS from scratch.  You'll be dependent upon someone with
VMWare Server or Workstation.  VMWare Server is a free product, and it
allows you to play as well as install.  If one were to ask me for
advice, I'd say "forget VMPlayer; get VMWare Server instead".

VMPlayer does have its uses, for example in a lab setting.  But using
it to play an already-existing guest OS is ludicrously simple, and I
don't think it's what you're after anyway.  I say this based on the
fact that you were wondering which OS install CD to bring to create a
new guest OS.

Carlos Konstanski

Install-time problems are always tough.  One thing to try: turn off
acpi and local apic at boot time.  If you can adjust your kernel
command line at the boot prompt, add "pci=noacpi nolapic".  I usually
use "nolapic" to get a machine with and ATI video card to make it
through an X11 installer like redhat.

Carlos Konstanski


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun Feb 18 21:26:42 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sun, 18 Feb 2007 21:26:42 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] VMplayer at the linux clinic
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0702181221160.11861@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <20070218063324.GA12488@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<45D8A471.3030703@comcast.net>
	<20070218111805.254b93b9.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0702181221160.11861@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <20070219052642.GA32376@gate.kl-ic.com>


To the potential clinic attendee who asked about VMware installs
at the clinic, sorry, I had left by 11.  I was busy helping other
folks all day, so I might not have had time to work on VMware 
anyway.  Perhaps we need to schedule an alternate time for VMware
installs.  Does someone want to organize this, and find a venue?


On Sun, Feb 18, 2007 at 12:29:57PM -0700, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> Just thought I'd mention that VMPlayer will not help you to install a
> new guest OS from scratch.  You'll be dependent upon someone with
> VMWare Server or Workstation.  VMWare Server is a free product, and it
> allows you to play as well as install.  If one were to ask me for
> advice, I'd say "forget VMPlayer; get VMWare Server instead".

This is obsolete information.  While I have experimented with Server
and Workstation, I did not need either product to create the vmdk
and vmx files necessary to create fully functional vmplayer guest
instances.  I just used easyvmx.com. which generates them for 
download after filling in a web form.  There are half a dozen sites
like easyvmx.com that offer similar services, and some might be
better, but I've done about 10 guests with easyvmx and I'll stick
with what works . I don't know whether they use a vmware product to
generate these files, but I could easily imagine a simple script
could do it.

It does help to have a copy of vmware tools, which does not come
with vmplayer, but you can get the tools from one of the demo
install disks for the spendy products, or download it from some
places on the net, or get it from a friend.  It is mostly a few
megabytes of better drivers for the vmware virtual peripherals.

In my experience, the beta versions of the products are crippleware,
which is why I don't use them.  They are instrumented with all sorts
of debug stuff, and are very slow compared to VMplayer.  YMMV.

The biggest problem with vmware so far is that the installer wants
to set you up with the wrong kind of networking.  VMware offers 
three kinds of networking, NAT, host-only, and bridged.  IMHO,
the best for most applications is host-only with NAT, but the
installer has a strong bias to build bridged networks.  That is
true (so far) of Server, Workstation, and VMWare player. 

When designing a Windoze guest, it is better to use a small vmdk
virtual disk, and to keep as much of the Windoze data and programs
in the Linux file system, and let Samba share them with Windoze.
That keeps the vmdk files small.  This means setting up Samba
fairly early in the guest install process.   However, when you 
are done, many Windoze programs will install entirely in the
mounted share, which means multiple guests can share the same
programs and data.  With enough memory and CPU for the host, it is
possible to run a "Windows Server" that supports perhaps 10 or 20
Windows guests, each run over X from other physical computers 
on the LAN, all using the same copy of a program.  If the program
is old enough, the program's EULA may not forbid this kind of
multiple simultaneous access.  

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> In my experience, the beta versions of the products are crippleware,
> which is why I don't use them.  They are instrumented with all sorts
> of debug stuff, and are very slow compared to VMplayer.  YMMV.
>   
Yeah ... don't use the beta versions for anything except finding out if 
your old use cases still work with the new version. They are otherwise 
useless.

VMware Server is OK -- it's essentially a cleaned-up version of VMware 
GSX. But GSX itself is a stripped version of Workstation, with some 
"server-like" features. The guest is essentially a parasite -- it runs 
as "root" and all of the guest's user or system time clocks to the 
host's *system* time!! You get what you pay for.

My advice: buy ESX or download Xen. GSX or VMware Server is more trouble 
than it's worth. I have a Windows-host Workstation 5.5 license, which I 
am not planning to upgrade to 6.0.

From krisa at subtend.net  Mon Feb 19 00:11:55 2007

Subject: [PLUG] VMplayer at the linux clinic

M. Edward (Ed) Borasky wrote:
> Keith Lofstrom wrote:
>> In my experience, the beta versions of the products are crippleware,
>> which is why I don't use them.  They are instrumented with all sorts

krisa at hooloovoo:~$ vmware -v
VMware Server 1.0.1 build-29996

krisa at hooloovoo:~$ ps -ef ww|grep vmx
vmware    5790     1  3 Feb01 ?        S<sl 849:48
/usr/local/lib/vmware/bin/vmware-vmx -C /var/vm/trillian2/trillian2.vmx
-@ ""
vmware    6634     1  1 Feb02 ?        S<sl 414:25
/usr/local/lib/vmware/bin/vmware-vmx -C /var/vm/zaphod/zaphod.vmx -@ ""

I have production vmware server installations humming along just fine.
As you can see they run as the "vmware" user.  The trick is chown'ing
the directory where your vm's will live to the user, and logging into
the server console as that user.  New guests will be created and run as
that user.  You can even chown different guests to different users who
can manage them separately through the console.

One installation has an uptime of 134 days (two guests: one
debian/sarge, one Windows 2003 Server).  No issues.

> My advice: buy ESX or download Xen. GSX or VMware Server is more trouble
> than it's worth. I have a Windows-host Workstation 5.5 license, which I
> am not planning to upgrade to 6.0.

I was ready to go with vmware infrastructure for a new deployment to
give it a try.   I backed out quickly when I found out VI3 could only be
fully managed using a Windows-only application (the web interface is not
full management).  Ooo this boiled my blood.  If you need it,
VirtualCenter looks like it requires production Windows servers to be
maintained.

So for an Ubuntu/Debian-only guest environment I need redundant Windows
server infrastructure and the talent (and finger crossing) to maintain
it?  Pass.

Another small irritation is VI3 forces an OS on you (RedHat).

XenEnterprise does this too (forcing a RedHat variant as underlying OS).
 Yes, yes, tuned environment, support, etc, etc.

Vmware server runs great on dapper and sarge, though it is not FOSS.
Eventually once KVM is baked and part of Ubuntu, I'll start testing.  I
have 2216 model Opteron's waiting for a stable KVM installation.  I
thought about compiling KVM for dapper, but I'm not convinced it's baked
yet, and the latest dapper misses VT extension detection by one version
(>=2.6.16.. dapper=2.6.15).

Oh, if you use LV's for you vmware guests like I do, be sure to allocate
enough disk space for the flat disk file *and* for a file equal to the
memory you allocate.  I got burned by the generation of a 2G .vmem file
equal to the 2G of RAM I gave the guest.

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

VMware Server is OK -- it's essentially a cleaned-up version of VMware 
GSX. But GSX itself is a stripped version of Workstation, with some 
"server-like" features. The guest is essentially a parasite -- it runs 
as "root" and all of the guest's user or system time clocks to the 
host's *system* time!! You get what you pay for.

My advice: buy ESX or download Xen. GSX or VMware Server is more trouble 
than it's worth. I have a Windows-host Workstation 5.5 license, which I 
am not planning to upgrade to 6.0.

On Sun, Feb 18, 2007 at 12:29:57PM -0700, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> Just thought I'd mention that VMPlayer will not help you to install a
> new guest OS from scratch.  You'll be dependent upon someone with
> VMWare Server or Workstation.  VMWare Server is a free product, and it
> allows you to play as well as install.  If one were to ask me for
> advice, I'd say "forget VMPlayer; get VMWare Server instead".

This is obsolete information.  While I have experimented with Server
and Workstation, I did not need either product to create the vmdk
and vmx files necessary to create fully functional vmplayer guest
instances.  I just used easyvmx.com. which generates them for 
download after filling in a web form.  There are half a dozen sites
like easyvmx.com that offer similar services, and some might be
better, but I've done about 10 guests with easyvmx and I'll stick
with what works . I don't know whether they use a vmware product to
generate these files, but I could easily imagine a simple script
could do it.

It does help to have a copy of vmware tools, which does not come
with vmplayer, but you can get the tools from one of the demo
install disks for the spendy products, or download it from some
places on the net, or get it from a friend.  It is mostly a few
megabytes of better drivers for the vmware virtual peripherals.

In my experience, the beta versions of the products are crippleware,
which is why I don't use them.  They are instrumented with all sorts
of debug stuff, and are very slow compared to VMplayer.  YMMV.

The biggest problem with vmware so far is that the installer wants
to set you up with the wrong kind of networking.  VMware offers 
three kinds of networking, NAT, host-only, and bridged.  IMHO,
the best for most applications is host-only with NAT, but the
installer has a strong bias to build bridged networks.  That is
true (so far) of Server, Workstation, and VMWare player. 

When designing a Windoze guest, it is better to use a small vmdk
virtual disk, and to keep as much of the Windoze data and programs
in the Linux file system, and let Samba share them with Windoze.
That keeps the vmdk files small.  This means setting up Samba
fairly early in the guest install process.   However, when you 
are done, many Windoze programs will install entirely in the
mounted share, which means multiple guests can share the same
programs and data.  With enough memory and CPU for the host, it is
possible to run a "Windows Server" that supports perhaps 10 or 20
Windows guests, each run over X from other physical computers 
on the LAN, all using the same copy of a program.  If the program
is old enough, the program's EULA may not forbid this kind of
multiple simultaneous access.  

Keith

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Mon Feb 19 15:59:50 2007
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)

Subject: [PLUG] VMplayer at the linux clinic

References: <848340.20326.qm@web509.biz.mail.mud.yahoo.com>

> so you say networking is better in the runtime version? what is the runtime version?
>
> alan <alan at clueserver.org> wrote: On Mon, 19 Feb 2007, Pomeroy Lab wrote:
>
>> I like server because I have never needed more. I can bridge my Server directly to the outside and have a field day with it. I really can't justify moving to Workstation for my personal needs. I have only used Server and Player but they both seem to work just fine.
>>
>
>
> I use Server to construct VMWare instances and then run them with the
> runtime version.  Seems to work much smoother.  (Server acts like it is
> trying to funnel everything through a socket connection.)

If you use VMWare server to run your VMs, and you run the VMs on one
machine while connecting to them from another machine, you get two
huge wins:

1. The VMs will stay running even if you disconnect the console
client.

2. The massive overhead of rendering the X display is offloaded to the
client machine, thereby making the VM run a LOT faster.

Carlos Konstanski

. I turned "off" Java "and" Javascript, then I visited the page too.

. Using Firefox on a WinXP PC, after loading part of the page & two
... images partway, it temporarily halted my PC too.

. The last time I saw something like happen, it was because the web page
... I was loading had an image (or something) on a "nonexistent" ip addr.
... Not sure, it "may" have also been both of the name servers were down.

. Sometimes your browser will tell you which site is dead at the bottom
... of the page, saying something like "loading from www.abcdefg.com".
... Keep your eyes open, and quickly jot down any IPs or URLs.

. I've seen this happen on both Firefox "and" Internet Explorer.

. I always think twice before creating a page which displays content
... from elsewhere.

Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> Just thought I'd mention that VMPlayer will not help you to install a
> new guest OS from scratch.  You'll be dependent upon someone with
> VMWare Server or Workstation.  VMWare Server is a free product, and it
> allows you to play as well as install.  If one were to ask me for
> advice, I'd say "forget VMPlayer; get VMWare Server instead".
>
I found a website (http://www.easyvmx.com/index.shtml) that creates the 
needed pieces (.vmx and virtual disk) to allow you to install from CD's 
into player.

After the creation process, you download, unarchive into a directory, 
and run vmplayer with the created vmx. If the cd or iso is mounted, the 
vm boots from it, and off you go.

Russ

using 'cat'.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue Feb 20 08:31:09 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 20 Feb 2007 08:31:09 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Turning off cups broadcast
Message-ID: <20070220163109.GA3516@gate.kl-ic.com>


Cups has a neat feature called autodiscovery, which allows computers
to share printers by default.  This feature is turned on by default,
no doubt so things "just work" for newbies.

In my case, I don't want to advertise my printer queues from my
machines, especially my laptop while roaming.  There are some lines
in /etc/cups/cupsd.conf:
---------------------------------------------------------------------
#
# Browsing: whether or not to broadcast and/or listen for CUPS printer
# information on the network.  Enabled by default.
#

#Browsing On

----------------------------------------------------------------------

One would think that adding the line:

----------------------------------------------------------------------
Browsing Off
----------------------------------------------------------------------

... would turn off the feature, but NO.   It keeps on spewing discovery
packets on port 631 to the broadcast address.  I finally turned this off
with the line:

----------------------------------------------------------------------
BrowseInterval  0
----------------------------------------------------------------------

Perhaps this will cause something odd to fail, like the guest printers
on my vmware host-only network.   But it is annoying sometimes;  I find
the damnedest printers popping up in /var/log/cups/error_log from while
roaming, no doubt from all the other laptops on the wireless broadcasting
their printers to the world.  At Linux clinic on Sunday, somebody was
broadcasting  store_4000  and  monkeyhouse,  for example.  Perhaps I
should have sent them a ransom note that would print when they got home.

On my home network,  I have iptables cranked up to high paranoia with
logging, and I was annoyed to see the broadcast packets from the laptop.
It took a while to figure out where they were coming from.   Hence this
message, for all the rest of you wondering where the ipp (port 631)
packets are coming from.

Keith

---
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs


From mcubed at slashmail.org  Tue Feb 20 08:33:47 2007
From: mcubed at slashmail.org (Michael M.)
Date: Tue, 20 Feb 2007 08:33:47 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Problems Installing Debian Sarge On Laptop
In-Reply-To: <45D8B7FB.8020203@comcast.net>
References: <45D8B7FB.8020203@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <1171989227.6164.26.camel@localhost>

On Sun, 2007-02-18 at 12:32 -0800, Dr. Terry L. Bonner wrote:
> Perhaps some wise soul can help.  I am attempting to install Debian 
> 3.14r on my HP Pavilion ze4230.  The process hangs on the select 
> language screen, when the setup stops responding to keyboard commands.
> 
> In the past, I have successfully installed both Mandriva and Ubuntu on 
> this laptop.  The keyboard is a ps2, as this old laptop's original 
> keyboard is kaput.
> 
> I am assuming that the hangup is caused by some hardware interface 
> problem, being as it occurs so early in the installation process.
> 
> Any help would be greatly appreciated.
> 
> I've always wanted to configure a debian box, as this is some sort of 
> rite of passage.  However, I'm not off to a promising start.


Any reason why you're trying to install Sarge and not Etch?  I ask only
because the release of Etch is imminent and the upgrade from Sarge to
Etch can be tricky (hotplug => udev, monolithic xf86 => modular xorg,
etc.).  Etch is very stable at this point and has been for quite awhile
-- it's in feature freeze, so the only updates are fixing RC bugs.  The
Etch RC1 installer released in November [1] breaks because of key
expiry, except for the full installation CDs and DVDs, but you can use
one of the [2] daily built images if you want to do a net install.  The
last time I tried it, the graphical mode didn't work for me (it hung
somewhere, I forget where), but the text (ncurses) mode worked just
fine.  The Etch RC2 installer should be out any day now.

If you can install Etch, you could save yourself some potential upgrade
trouble.  And at this point I don't think the Debian devs are interested
in Sarge installer bugs -- all the emphasis is on getting Etch released.

[1] http://www.debian.org/devel/debian-installer/News/2007/20070210
[2] http://www.debian.org/devel/debian-installer/


-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA

of absolute reality; even larks and katydids are supposed, by some, to
dream." --S. Jackson

I have actually installed a new OS using VMPlayer.  I built the raw HD
image with
qemu and a vmx file that was configured for a boot CD/DVD.  After running the
install from the CD/DVD.  I changed the configuration file to not look
for the iso file and
things just booted from the HD image.  I installed FC4 this way using
minimum boot iso
doing a net install and Ubuntu,Freespire, OpenSuse and Slax  with a
LiveCD install.
These worked and then I was able to run the update tools from within
and get the
updates to that image.

References: <86y7mtw6bt.fsf@coulee.tdb.com> <45DB0E0E.2020201@cesmail.net>
Message-ID: <86ejokwxgh.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "M" == M Edward (Ed) Borasky <znmeb at cesmail.net> writes:

Russell> Okay, this has nothing to do with Linux, per se, but I've
Russell> received some fortuitous assistance here from the
Russell> mathematically gifted, so I'll try again.  I want to randomly
Russell> select points with a uniform probability over the surface of
Russell> an an ellipsoid (the WGS84 ellipsoid representing the earth,
Russell> to be concrete about it).  I know how to do it for spheres,
Russell> but I have yet to find anything equally comprehensible for an
Russell> ellipsoid.

Russell> Any clues?  Pointers?

M> There might be something built into R for that -- there are quite a
M> few geospatial contributed packages. My recollection is that the
M> Earth is "close enough" to a sphere that your sphere technique
M> might work anyway. :)

It may be "close enough", but I don't want people to be able to
challenge the validity of a random sample, so I am trying to go the
extra mile.

M> How do you do it for a sphere? 

  double ra1 = drand48();
  double ra2 = drand48();
  double theta = ra1 * 2 * pi;

  double dz = (ra2 - 0.5) * 2;
  double dxy = sqrt(1 - dz * dz);

  double dx=dxy * cos(theta);
  double dy=dxy * sin(theta);


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Tue Feb 20 09:52:35 2007
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Tue, 20 Feb 2007 09:52:35 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] [OT] Ellipsoid uniform random distribution
In-Reply-To: <86ejokwxgh.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <86y7mtw6bt.fsf@coulee.tdb.com> <45DB0E0E.2020201@cesmail.net>
	<86ejokwxgh.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0702200938350.23154@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Tue, 20 Feb 2007, Russell Senior wrote:

> Any clues?  Pointers?

   There is a good discussion of geoid, ellipsoids, and various datums in
USGS Professional Paper 1395[1]. Pages 11-19 cover the subject
comprehensively and include many formulae.

   Useful points: in many commercial atlases it may be assumed that the Earth
is a sphere, but it is more nearly an oblate ellipsoid.

   The geoid is the name given to the shape that the Earth would assume if it
were all measured at mean sea level. Elevations and contour lines on the
Earth are reported relative to the geoid, not the ellipsoid. Latitude,
longitude, and all plane coordinate systems, on the other hand, are
determined with respect to the ellipsoid.

   The GRS 80 ellipsoid has an equatorial radius (a) of 6,378,137 meters, a
polar radius (b) of 6,356,752.3 meters, and a flattening (f) of 1/298.257.

   The WGS 72 ellipsoid as a = 6,378,135, b = 6,356,750.5, and f = 1/298.26.

   The PSU library probably has a copy.

Rich

[1] Snyder, John F. 1987. Map Projections -- A Working Manual. USGPO,
Washington, DC.

Rich> datums in USGS Professional Paper 1395[1]. Pages 11-19 cover the

Rich>    The geoid is the name given to the shape that the Earth would
Rich> assume if it were all measured at mean sea level. Elevations and
Rich> contour lines on the Earth are reported relative to the geoid,
Rich> not the ellipsoid.

Yeah, but my GPS device and google maps and pretty much everything
else I care about for this application use WGS84.  WGS84 is a=6378137
meters, f=1/298.257223563, where f = (a - b)/a, a is the equitorial
radius, and b is the polar radius (along the minor axis).

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue Feb 20 12:16:09 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 20 Feb 2007 12:16:09 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Removable Hard Disk Recommendations
In-Reply-To: <B0031645690@bct-mail.bctonline.com>

Message-ID: <20070220201609.GB6055@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Tue, Feb 20, 2007 at 11:33:42AM -0800, Charles Sliger wrote:
> I want to build a server with at least 1 removable hard disk for use in
> backups.
> 
> I've heard that there can be reliability issues with the disk carriers.

I have been sliding disks in and out of a ViPower Ultra-ATA disk
rack daily for about three years now and it has only needed
replacement once.  They do wear out;  get a spare.  Unless you 
can get something with thick gold plating on the connectors, it
will wear out.

OTOH, the big issue is a version of Linux that supports PATA hot
swap.  That was in the later 2.4.xx kernels, but it was removed
from 2.6.xx for at least a while.  It might be back in.  Perhaps
SATA is better supported.  I don't know whether the carriers for
SATA are reliable.

Keith

From alan at clueserver.org  Tue Feb 20 12:39:52 2007
From: alan at clueserver.org (alan)
Date: Tue, 20 Feb 2007 12:39:52 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Removable Hard Disk Recommendations
In-Reply-To: <B0031645690@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
References: <B0031645690@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0702201235290.29667@blackbox.fnordora.org>

On Tue, 20 Feb 2007, Charles Sliger wrote:

> I want to build a server with at least 1 removable hard disk for use in
> backups.
>
> I've heard that there can be reliability issues with the disk carriers.
>
> Any insights welcome.

USB or plug in tray?

The advantage to USB is that it is a hot plug device.  (You don't have to 
shut down to remove it.)

I have not had problems with either as long as you don't buy the extra 
cheap versions.  (Those tend to cut corners and have problems -- 
sometimes.)

The only other issue is if you reboot with a usb drive plugged in, it may 
try to boot off of that disc.  The easiest way to fix that is to configure 
the boot order with the USB device plugged in in the BIOS.  (If it is not 
plugged in, some bioses will not give you the option to change the boot 
order for USB devices.)

Hope that helps.

I opted to go the external USB 2.0 route and got the Seagate 160GB 7200
rpm drive. I rsync...
/home
/var/spool/mail
/etc
/var/www
/var/log/httpd
/usr/local/bin
...and the 'My Documents' folder mounted on my XP box nightly (with
rotating hard links to provide historical backups sets).

Filesystem            Size  Used Avail Use% Mounted on
/dev/sdb1             147G   60G   81G  43% /media/usbdisk
//sandy/docs          299G   43G  256G  15% /windocs

The drive was recognized instantly on my RHEL (soon to be CentOS)
web/mail server (IBM x206) and formatted easily as ext3. It's been a
trouble-free solution for me.

> OTOH, the big issue is a version of Linux that supports PATA hot

On Tue, Feb 20, 2007 at 01:00:19PM -0800, Charles Sliger wrote:
> [chaz> ] This system will be running CentOS-4. Do you know if it supports
> hot swap?

Not PATA up to 2.6.18 kernels.  Probably USB.  Maybe SATA.  The
relevant phrase above is "I don't know", so you will have to 
research it and let us know.

On 2.4.22 (FC1), you can remove a disk with "hdparm -b 0 /dev/hdc".  
With 2.6.9 (RHEL4) and 2.6.18 (RHEL5beta) you get the error message:

   HDIO_SET_BUSSTATE failed: Operation not supported

Now you know what I do.  Please find out more and share with us.  I
am especially interested in whether SATA will hotswap properly;  SATA
disks are cheap and big, and the Wave Of The Future according to some.

Keith

You don't want *-ia64.  You want *-amd64.  And you don't want
Debian-3.1r4-* -- that's Sarge (stable), soon to be "old stable."

supplied and click on the netinst CD image for amd64.

A netinst install is not necessarily a slow thing.  I installed debian
(recently) on a VMWare guest with a netinst CD.  The entire install,
including a kernel download and compile (I never use distro kernels)
took an hour.  This included google time, as my custom kernel did not
boot until I figured out which SCSI driver to use for disk access
(BusLogic in my case).  My internet access was throttled to 800K/s
download speed; compare that to the 1M/s I get at home on my cable
modem.  I would not call this a long install by any means (of course,
I'm accustomed to gentoo; a slow install in my book is one that takes
more than 4 days).  A proper debian install is minimal anyway - you
are not downloading a whole lot.  The sources.list file you get at
install time is spartan; you won't even be able to get much cool stuff
until you replace it with your favorite sources.  You'll spend a lot
more time in apt-get later than during the install.  Plus there's the
up-to-date-ness factor.  Netinst will get you the latest packages.  If
you use an even slightly out-of-date bloater CD, how many of those
packages are you going to reinstall right out of the chute, as soon as
you do your first "apt-get dist-upgrade"?

Carlos Konstanski

> I'm accustomed to gentoo; a slow install in my book is one that takes

> packages are you going to reinstall right out of the chute, as soon as
> you do your first "apt-get dist-upgrade"?


Carlos is right -- if you were to download a dated full CD or DVD image
and install a lot of packages from it, you'd have a lot of updating to
do, which would be painful over a slow connection.  The fastest way to
go is download a netinst .iso and do a minimal installation (no xorg, no
DEs or WMs).  Downloading packages won't take very long at all, because
you're only downloading a relatively small number.  Configure, reboot,
then start adding packages as needed.  The problem with choosing
"Desktop Environment" from the installer is that it downloads both KDE
and Gnome.  That takes quite a while, even with a fast broadband, and
it's probably more than you want.

Once you've rebooted, fire up aptitude and look at the "Tasks" options.
>From there you can select *either* Gnome *or* KDE (or XFCE), if you want
a full desktop environment.  It takes much less time to download one or
the other than it does to download both.

Or, even better, issue one of:

~$ aptitude install xorg gnome-desktop-environment
~$ aptitude install xorg kde
~$ aptitude install xorg xfce4

If you don't want a DE, you can just install xorg and whatever window
manager strikes your fancy.  (You probably also want a terminal, like
xterm or aterm, not to mention a graphical web browser.  The minimal
install includes w3m.)  That will be fairly quick.  It has taken me
roughly 45 minutes to download xorg & gnome-desktop-environment at about
160kB/s.  Obviously, just downloading xorg & [flux/open/black]box or
icewm, plus a terminal, graphical web browser, and assorted apps will be
faster.

I'm not sure what you mean about the server being popular.  I just use
the generic "deb http://ftp.us.debian.org/debian/ etch main non-free
contrib" in my sources.list and I always get a fast mirror -- that is,
one that maxes out my download capacity.  Security downloads are equally
as fast.

The "amd64" image is for 64-bit Intel and AMD CPUs.  There's no separate
image for Intel.  Debian calls it "amd64" because AMD was first to
market with 64-bit.  (Debian also call the 32-bit installer "i386" even
though it runs on AMD 32-bit CPUs.)  Some distros refer to the
architecture as "x86_64," but it's all the same.  Personally, I think
the "x86_64" moniker is kind of a slight to AMD.  After all, "x86"
orginally denoted processors developed by Intel.  So AMD comes along and
develops a 64-bit extension, and Intel's terminology sticks?  Not fair.

But I don't loose any sleep over the issue.  :-)


-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA

1. There may be more AMD 64-bit machines running
64-bit Linux than Intel. Does anyone have stats
on this?

2. Why shouldn't we give credit to the creator of
the technology? Using "x86" in the name gives credit
to Intel. I surveyed my students one day about this.
In a class of about 20, _everyone_ said that Intel
created the x86_64 64-bit technology.

3. I think we need to go out of our way to make it
clear when technologies are NOT created by market
leaders, such as Intel, and monopolists, such as
Microsoft. Sort of an affirmative action program.
Far too often, people give Microsoft credit for
technologies developed by others. In another survey,
most of my students said that Microsoft created SQL.
Only one gave a different answer with a smile that
suggested that the other students were dummies and
he knew the answer; he said Oracle.

Ed

I'm considering a desktop replacement and have just recently begun to 
consider a laptop.

I think I would like to set up a home workstation with my new fancy 
widescreen display working in tandem with a laptop display (with my laptop 
in something like a book holder) and external USB keyboard and mouse. 
Then, if necessary, I can take the whole shebang to go.

My desktop machine isn't a really heavy number cruncher (at the moment), 
so I can get away with probably the most minimal thing that will do the 
dual display (at native resolution for both the LCDs).  I don't really do 
anything more intensive than video playback and audio editting on this 
machine.

If I go the laptop route, I will probably want some REALLY cheap little 
machine that I can load with drives to be a home-based network storage 
device.

Some people have mentioned getting really cheap laptops on Ebay.  How many 
generations old are the good, cheap ones?  (i.e., where's the sweet spot 
for price/performance?)

I recently built a machine for someone and was upset to find that there 
was no SATA support for Linux and had to return the HD and get a PATA one. 
Is this common?  What about SATA external enclosures?

I'm hoping that my home fileserver will do both NFS (for my desktop 
machine) and SMB (for the XBox).  The machine I'm using for that purpose 
now is just totally unsuitable given my requirements for disk space.

J.

From mcubed at slashmail.org  Wed Feb 21 16:25:48 2007
From: mcubed at slashmail.org (Michael M.)
Date: Wed, 21 Feb 2007 16:25:48 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Learned something new ... dpkg, aptitude
Message-ID: <1172103948.30978.20.camel@localhost>

So I followed a tip I read to generate a list of all packages I have
installed:

$ dpkg --get-selections >> installed.packages.list.txt

In looking over the list, I saw several entries for packages with a
status of "deinstall" -- but it certainly didn't list everything I had
installed then uninstalled.  This was puzzling.

Then I read another tip, about using aptitude's package tree limit with
the search pattern ~c.  This will show you all the packages for which
you have configuration files hanging around, even though the package is
no longer installed.

And whaddya know, it matches the entries in my installed packages list
that show a status of "deinstall."

Now I realize what these are:  they are packages that aptitude removed
in the course of upgrades, stuff like gstreamer0.8-flac.  Normally, when
I uninstall something I use the "purge" option, which removes
configuration files, but when aptitude removes something in the course
of an upgrade, it leaves the configs.

One small step on the road leading out of Newbieville.  :-)


-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA
"No live organism can continue for long to exist sanely under conditions
of absolute reality; even larks and katydids are supposed, by some, to
dream." --S. Jackson

From aaron at bavariati.org  Thu Feb 22 09:34:45 2007
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Thu, 22 Feb 2007 09:34:45 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Laptops and storage
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0702211530300.8947@shaft.bitmine.net>
References: <29845.170.135.112.12.1172099570.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0702211530300.8947@shaft.bitmine.net>
Message-ID: <20070222173445.GD23465@syrinxpc.com>

On Wed, Feb 21, 2007 at 03:40:04PM -0800, Jeme A Brelin wrote:
> I think I would like to set up a home workstation with my new fancy 
> widescreen display working in tandem with a laptop display (with my laptop 
> in something like a book holder) and external USB keyboard and mouse. 
> Then, if necessary, I can take the whole shebang to go.

Laptops with dual-VGA and docking stations are indeed lovely things.
Just be careful what video chipset the lappie uses so you can make sure
you get dual-head support in X.

> If I go the laptop route, I will probably want some REALLY cheap little 
> machine that I can load with drives to be a home-based network storage 
> device.

NAS appliances are quite inexpensive these days.

> How many generations old are the good, cheap ones?  (i.e., where's the
> sweet spot for price/performance?)

That depends entirely on what you want.  Make a wishlist, set a budget,
buy carefully.  You can get brand-new lappies for $500-$1000, and there
may still be some good pre-Vista ones floating around at bargain prices.
If you want a little cheaper, you can get used IBM T-series lapdogs from
local retailers for $300-$600.

My own wishlist is: 
* Built-in Mini-PCI WiFi (replace w/a Senao?)
* Dualhead supported under X
* Docking station
* Batteries and power supplies commonly available
* No bigger than 10"x12"x1.5"
* TrackPoint
* Kernel VM support (Intel VT or AMD SVM)
* 512+ MB RAM

Currently, I'm looking at ~$1,200 for most of what I want.  Since I have
an adequate laptop right now, my money has more important things to do,
mostly involving motorcycles and/or financial stability.

> I recently built a machine for someone and was upset to find that there 
> was no SATA support for Linux and had to return the HD and get a PATA one. 
> Is this common?  What about SATA external enclosures?

Newer Linux distros have good native SATA support.  If your distro
doesn't support your motherboard's SATA controller, you can usually go
into BIOS setup and configure the controller to operate in "compatible"
mode.  This makes the SATA controller look like a PATA controller.

> I'm hoping that my home fileserver will do both NFS (for my desktop 
> machine) and SMB (for the XBox).  The machine I'm using for that purpose 
> now is just totally unsuitable given my requirements for disk space.

Huh.  Can you elaborate on that?  You could get a TeraStation, NSLU2 or
something.  Or just buy a $25 Linux box at FreeGeek and stuff drives in.

   A discussion with a very helpful young woman at Easy Software provided me
with the solution to the non-duplex printing. It required an upgrade (free,
too!) from PrintPro-4.4.1 to -4.5.12 for the 2.6.x kernels. Whew! Yea, team!

   Also, in case anyone else uses the PrintPro PPD drivers, they work only
with CUPS-1.1.x. While the cups developers rush forward (they're up to
-1.2.28 now), the PrintPro developers are lagging behind.

   Anyway, it woiks now and I'm happy to drop this off the fixit list.

I've been reading this thread because I have a similar problem. I
haven't said anything because I have no solution.

I have a Laserjet 5SiMx (identical to the one at Free Geek) and a
Laserjet 8000DN (physically the same printer, but HP changed all the
electronics). Both have duplexers. Duplexing always worked fine with
both until after the upgrade from Dapper to Edgy. Now I can still
duplex to the 8000DN, but the 5SiMx is broken. I've tried everything
available, PostScript, gutenprint, CUPS, command line with lp and lpr,
HPLIP, but no joy. Duplexing is set on the printer control panel as the
default and it is also set as the default in CUPS. And I select it in
the application print dialog box as well. But it still prints single
sided.

So if you work on the HPLIP team and know anything about this, please
share!

On Thu, 22 Feb 2007 15:10:37 -0800
Aaron <ke7ezt at gmail.com> dijo:

> I just tested a LaserJet 5SiM and the duplex worked correctly.
> 
> Although in cups I changed:
> 
> Duplex Unit (for 2-Sided Printing): to Installed
> 
> and
> 
> 2-Sided Printing: to Short Edge Binding.
> 
> I printed from openoffice with no problems. What application are you
> printing from?

Hey! It's working now!

I have no idea why. I spent hours the other day trying to get it to
print duplexed from several different apps, and nothing worked. I
didn't make any changes just now either.

<rant mode: On>
Part of the problem (in Windows also) is that there are three places to
change settings: 

- the printer's control panel
- CUPS properties for the printer
- the application print dialog box

And most applications offer a button to change the options for the
printer as well. This button should change the options temporarily in
CUPS, but it does not open the CUPS properties window. So God only
knows what that button really changes.

In theory, settings in the printer's control panel should be overridden
by the settings in CUPS, and settings in CUPS should be overridable in
the application print dialog box. In reality, that theory is regularly
ignored and violated by just about every application. And often the
same option is available in different places in the application print
dialog box, and one setting counters the other. Either that or the
option just doesn't work.

For example, in the OpenOffice.org main print dialog box there is a
Properties button next to the printer drop-down box. With the Laserjet
5SiMx PostScript printer selected, when you click on the Properties
button you get a dialog box which has an option for Paper Tray, which
is set to 3 for my Laserjet 5SiMx. The main dialog box also has a
button labeled Options, which pops up a different dialog box that has a
checkbox for Paper Tray from Printer Settings. Meantime, I always have
the printer's control panel set to use whatever paper tray has paper in
it (Automatic setting in the printer control panel). 

So if I check the Paper Tray from Printer Settings from the printer
Options dialog box it will always try to pull from the manual feed
tray, which is always empty because I never use it, thus the print job
hangs until I walk over and hit the Select button to make it pull
from another tray. The Paper Tray option on the other printer
Properties popup window says Tray 3. I cannot change it to Automatic
because that option is not listed. I can change it to 1, 2, 3 or 4. But
whatever I set it to doesn't matter. As soon as I close that popup
window and reopen it the setting is back to Tray 3.

It has often been repeated that printing is one of the weak parts of
Linux, and its true. What we need is for apps to be forced to strict
compliance with CUPS. No fair trying to go around CUPS and change
printer settings directly. And where CUPS has an option for a printer
the application print dialog box should display the same option using
the same terminology and with the same options. At least that way if
there is a problem with a printer the CUPS people can fix it once and
it will be fixed for everything. 
<rant mode: Off> ... Well, until this computer does something else to
piss me off. :)

Hi Pluggers,
I started a thread about virtualization awhile back and got some great
information. After attempting to install VM Ware server on my Fedora Core 6
installation I keep running into a problem that Google has yet to solve for
me. I can install the rpm that VM Ware provides just fine. However when I
try and run vmware-config.pl it is unable to find my kernel headers which
are indeed installed (in my case in /usr/src/kernels/<kernel-ver> when I
point to that directory the config tool spews back that the header files are
not from my running kernel yet they are or at least they should be according
to the documentation I've read.

I have the following packages installed.

kernel-2.6.19-1.2895.fc6-i686
kernel-devel-2.6.19-1.2895.fc6-i686
kernel-headers-2.6.19-1.2895.fc6-i686

I have tried updating to the latest kernel from the update repo with no
success (same error message).

The one forum I found this specific issue on listed the problem as having to
do with the arch conflicting between the header/devel packages and the
kernel package but I've verified repeatedly that they match.


Anyone have any pointers/debugging tips?

Thanks,
Drew-

Hi all,

I have a tree on a filesystem I want to prune, but I'd like to save it to DVD 
or CD recordable media before doing so in case I need it in the future. I 
would then like to have a printed listing of files for "offline searching."

What I'd like to accomplish is burning a number of discs labelled 1-N and then 
have a printable (plaintext is fine) tree of which files are on which disc. 
Hopefully whatever software can do this would gracefully handle stuffing as 
many files as possible on each disc so I don't have to do this myself. I've 
tried, it's a PITA and I'm talking about 700+GB.

I gave google the usual chance but I may not know how to describe what I'm 
trying to find. Various permutations of linux burning and cataloging returned 
little of relevance.

Does anyone know of software like this or have any similar suggestions I might 
try?

-Aaron

From mcubed at slashmail.org  Sat Feb 24 14:10:16 2007
From: mcubed at slashmail.org (Michael M.)
Date: Sat, 24 Feb 2007 14:10:16 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] CD/DVD Burning and Cataloging
In-Reply-To: <200702241244.59736.aaron@madebyai.com>
References: <200702241244.59736.aaron@madebyai.com>
Message-ID: <1172355016.4092.9.camel@localhost>

On Sat, 2007-02-24 at 12:44 -0800, Aaron Ten Clay wrote:
> Hi all,
> 
> I have a tree on a filesystem I want to prune, but I'd like to save it to DVD 
> or CD recordable media before doing so in case I need it in the future. I 
> would then like to have a printed listing of files for "offline searching."
> 
> What I'd like to accomplish is burning a number of discs labelled 1-N and then

> trying to find. Various permutations of linux burning and cataloging returned 
> little of relevance.
> 
> Does anyone know of software like this or have any similar suggestions I might 
> try?
> 


I'm not sure I understand exactly what you're after, but did you check
[1] GTKtalog?

It won't auto-fill the discs for you, you'll have to find something else
for that.  But it will catalog every file on your discs in directory
tree fashion and provide a mechanism for browsing/searching through the
discs quickly.  Also has a less robust ncurses option, freeing you from
always needing X and GTK+.

I'm not sure if you can print directly from GTKtalog, but:

[2] "You can display file information with GTKtalog internal viewer or
with any other viewer you choose (mozilla, xemacs ..)."

So you can display it through something else that you can print from.

[1] http://www.nongnu.org/gtktalog/index.en.html
[2] http://www.nongnu.org/gtktalog/features.en.html


-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA

of absolute reality; even larks and katydids are supposed, by some, to
dream." --S. Jackson

numbered directories with a subset of the files in them, or even burns 
directly.

I just hit this after rebooting into a 2.6.19 kernel in Friday, but it
sounds like you've find the solution.  Here are a couple of general
pointers:

 o VMWare has self-support forums which are usually quite helpful.
 o A VMWare employee name Petr releases an unofficial set of patches
that usually handle this kind of thing.  His
'vmware-any-any-updateXXX.tar.gz' can be found here:
   http://platan.vc.cvut.cz/ftp/pub/vmware/

Why the hell is that?  We've finally gotten a sound subsystem that
people seem to be mostly happy with--ALSA--but mixing is still a total
mess.  My motherboard only has 3 ports--line in, line out and microphone
in (I think), so I'm not sure what all several dozen sliders are for.
(Well, that's the CD audio port too.)  While I know roughly what PCM is,
I don't understand why there is a separate slider for it, in addition to
"master" and "headphone."  The curses-based ALSA mixer is nearly
unusable in its unfriendliness and the graphical one actually hung
several times when I tried to use it (not to mention that it behaves
strangely at times, like the fact that the channels are unlocked by
default, instead of locked).  xfce4-mixer isn't much better;
gnome-volume-control kinda works.  Sometimes they even seem to control
different things, too.  I had to use Skype a few weeks ago for a
conference call and it took me *forever* to figure out how to turn up
the microphone input levels so it would actually record.  I'm still
kinda vague about what I actually did to change it.

From mcubed at slashmail.org  Sat Feb 24 19:54:54 2007
From: mcubed at slashmail.org (Michael M.)
Date: Sat, 24 Feb 2007 19:54:54 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] CD/DVD Burning and Cataloging
In-Reply-To: <200702241431.12203.aaron@madebyai.com>
References: <200702241244.59736.aaron@madebyai.com>
	<1172355016.4092.9.camel@localhost>
	<200702241431.12203.aaron@madebyai.com>
Message-ID: <1172375694.4092.17.camel@localhost>

On Sat, 2007-02-24 at 14:31 -0800, Aaron Ten Clay wrote:

> The part I see as more important is something which can, given a media size 
> (650MB, 700MB, 4.7GB, etc.) split the tree of files up so it can be burned 
> onto individual discs. I don't care if it creates ISOs, makes a bunch of 
> numbered directories with a subset of the files in them, or even burns 
> directly.


Any of these sound promising?:

(1)  http://cdbkup.sourceforge.net/ 

CDBKUP is a professional-grade open-source package for backing up
filesystems onto CD-Rs or CD-RWs. 
Features
      * Supports full or incremental backups.
      * Results are stored as GNU tarballs on ISO 9660 filesystems.
      * Excellent support for multi-session CDs.
      * Large backups can be split between multiple CDs.
      * Supports gzip, bzip2 or no compression.

(2) http://www.bluehaze.com.au/unix/cdbkup.html

Backup is a shell script that makes use of standard unix utilities such
as find, sed, and cpio, plus a small file-splitter called fsplit (#)
(source and Linux x86 executable included) to create a cpio backup
archive of your system. The archive is created on your hard drive in the
form of a series of files which can then be burned onto separate CDs or
DVDs.

The default fragment size is still currently set to around 640 Mb so
they can fit onto normal CDs, but as of version 4.20, this can be
changed via "-s " to suit common DVDs (eg: -s 4.7). The chunk files are
automatically named by backup by appending 000, 001 etc onto a
date-derived file name base.

(3) http://danborn.net/multicd/

Introduction 
I wanted to backup my entire Linux system. I wanted to do it by making
direct copies of the files (no archiving or compression), so if I just
wanted to look at one of the files on one of the CDs, I could. This also
makes it very easy to restore from backups. Backing up my entire Linux
system takes more than one CD, so I was running the du command on a
bunch of directories to see what would fit on which CD. This was very
tedious and time consuming, so I wrote multicd to do this work for me.


Description 
multicd has a fairly large number of options. It can be configured to
run in a variety of ways. Here are some of the more interesting
features:

multicd can run in an interactive or non-interactive mode.

      * Interactive mode assumes that you will be burning more than one
        disc. You tell multicd which files to backup, and it will prompt
        you to insert a disc each time it is ready to burn a new one.
      * Non-interactive mode copies the files to an image file, and then
        burns just a single CD. It assumes you have left a CD in your
        burner. This is useful if you want to call multicd from a script
        or a cron job.


-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA
"No live organism can continue for long to exist sanely under conditions
of absolute reality; even larks and katydids are supposed, by some, to
dream." --S. Jackson

                                 PRESENTATION

                What went wrong with my disaster recovery plan

                                      by

                                 Brian Martin
                         <Brian at martinconsulting.com>


      Although many organizations have disaster recovery plans,
      an actual disaster can present a number of unexpected problems.
      From transportation difficulties to incorrect public information
      to clueless vendors, Brian Martin will share the realities he
      experienced during an actual disaster in the aftermath of the
      1989 Loma Prieta earthquake.
      This non-technical presentation will be suitable to anyone
      involved in or even thinking about disaster recovery.

On Wed, 28 Feb 2007, Rich Shepard wrote:

>  Pine comes up in read-only mode. It says there's another process that has
> a lock on it. But, 'ps ax | grep pine' reveals only a single process, and no

From heinlein at madboa.com  Wed Feb 28 06:44:38 2007
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Wed, 28 Feb 2007 06:44:38 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Pine Issue
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0702280615310.8243@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0702280615310.8243@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.0.82.0702280644330.1290@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Wed, 28 Feb 2007, Rich Shepard wrote:

>  Pine comes up in read-only mode. It says there's another process 
> that has a lock on it. But, 'ps ax | grep pine' reveals only a 
> single process, and no one other than my one instance uses pine. 
> This started only last evening.
>
>  What might be the cause of this? More importantly, how do I get 
> normal operations back?

First, close pine and then see if any other processes have opened your 
mail spool:

    sudo lsof | grep /var/spool/mail

You may need to kill an errant process to regain a lock on your inbox.

If that turns up nada, look for /var/spool/mail/rshepard.lock or any 
file that looks and/or smells like a lock file -- and delete it.

NB: These examples assume your inbox is located in /var/spool/mail; 
adjust as necessary.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

From plug at merlot.com  Wed Feb 28 11:29:29 2007
From: plug at merlot.com (Kurt Sussman)
Date: Wed, 28 Feb 2007 11:29:29 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] postfix content_filter AFTER recipient_restrictions?
Message-ID: <20070228192929.GI11914@merlot.com>

I recently added amavis to my postfix configuration, and followed some
'filter ALL mail' instructions simply because I had a deadline and they
were handy.

Now I see that I really want to have more control over where in the
list of restrictions the content_filter falls. E.g. I want to check my
sender exceptions and rfc822 stuff, THEN apply the content_filter, then
check the rest of my restrictions.

Is this possible? Hints will be greatly appreciated...

Thanks.

--Kurt

    Merlot Research Group, Inc               http://www.merlot.com
    kls[at]merlot.com        GPG key 82505A74       GTalk: ratbelt


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Feb 28 12:25:29 2007
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 28 Feb 2007 12:25:29 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] postfix content_filter AFTER recipient_restrictions?
In-Reply-To: <20070228192929.GI11914@merlot.com>
References: <20070228192929.GI11914@merlot.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0702281218360.19522@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Wed, 28 Feb 2007, Kurt Sussman wrote:

> Now I see that I really want to have more control over where in the list
> of restrictions the content_filter falls. E.g. I want to check my sender
> exceptions and rfc822 stuff, THEN apply the content_filter, then check the
> rest of my restrictions.
>
> Is this possible? Hints will be greatly appreciated...

Kurt,

   Yes. The order in which the restrictions are listed are the order in which
they are checked. And, that makes a _very_ big difference.

   When I tried greylisting, I had the call to the greylist tool high in the
check list. _Every_ message was sent off. Then I moved it to the very end,
and only those that made it past all the other checks were greylisted. (Then
I removed it because it was more of a bother than a help. :-) )

   The general idea, IIRC, is to do all your acceptances first (e.g.,
permit_mynetworks), then apply the other filters. I also learned recently
that there are advantages of separating check_client_restrictions prior to
check_sender_restrictions. Even without the greylisting, I'm back down to
perhaps 4 spam per day that plop into my INBOX.

   My UCE filters are these; I don't use avavis, but you know where to insert
it in the queue:

smtpd_client_restrictions =
         check_client_access hash:/etc/postfix/internal_network,
         permit_mynetworks,
         #check_sender_access hash:/etc/postfix/sender_no_greylist,
         check_client_access hash:/etc/postfix/badaddr,
         reject_rbl_client zen.spamhaus.org,
         reject_rbl_client bl.spamcop.net,
         reject_rbl_client list.dsbl.org,
         reject_rhsbl_sender dsn.rfc-ignorant.org,
         reject_unknown_reverse_client_hostname,
         check_sender_mx_access cidr:/etc/postfix/bogus_mx,
         check_sender_access hash:/etc/postfix/rhsbl_sender_exceptions,
         check_sender_access hash:/etc/postfix/common_spam_senderdomains,
         check_sender_access hash:/etc/postfix/badaddr,
         permit

smtpd_recipient_restrictions =
         permit_mynetworks,
         reject_unauth_destination,
         check_recipient_access hash:/etc/postfix/roleaccount_exceptions,
         check_recipient_access hash:/etc/postfix/recipients,
         check_helo_access pcre:/etc/postfix/helo_checks,
         reject_non_fqdn_recipient,
         reject_non_fqdn_sender,
         reject_unknown_sender_domain,
         reject_non_fqdn_hostname,
         reject_invalid_hostname,
         permit

smtp_data_restrictions =
         reject_multi_recipient_bounce

HTH,

Rich

From mikeraz at patch.com  Wed Feb 28 14:37:46 2007
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Wed, 28 Feb 2007 14:37:46 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] vulnerable Linux
Message-ID: <45943.170.135.112.12.1172702266.squirrel@mail.patch.com>

In case you don't read LWN.

Securing Linux by breaking it with Damn Vulnerable Linux

Damn Vulnerable Linux (DVL) is everything a good Linux distribution isn't. Its
developers have spent hours stuffing it with broken, ill-configured, outdated, and
exploitable software that makes it vulnerable to attacks. DVL isn't built to run
on your desktop -- it's a learning tool for security students.


DVL is a live CD available as a 150MB ISO. It's based on the popular mini-Linux
distribution Damn Small Linux (DSL), not only for its minimal size, but also for
the fact that DSL uses a 2.4 kernel, which makes it easier to offer vulnerable
elements that might not work under the 2.6 kernel. It contains older, easily
breakable versions of Apache, MySQL, PHP, and FTP and SSH daemons, as well as
several tools available to help you compile, debug, and break applications running
on these services, including GCC, GDB, NASM, strace, ELF Shell, DDD, LDasm, LIDa,
and more.

http://www.linux.com/article.pl?sid=07/02/15/1747220

   Michael Rasmussen, Portland, Ore, USA

----- "Michael Rasmussen" <mikeraz at patch.com> wrote:
> In case you don't read LWN.
> 
> Securing Linux by breaking it with Damn Vulnerable Linux
> 

> DVL is a live CD available as a 150MB ISO. It's based on the popular
> mini-Linux
> distribution Damn Small Linux (DSL), not only for its minimal size,
> but also for
> the fact that DSL uses a 2.4 kernel, which makes it easier to offer
> vulnerable
> elements that might not work under the 2.6 kernel.


That seems kinda stupid to me, I would imagine the amount of relevant knowledge to be gained by people breaking into worn aged software is much smaller than the insight that would be gained by a 2.6 kernel with lots of software not too old running on it.

It's like saying there's tons to be gained by watching people hack windows 95. Your also much more likely to get a "false" positive (script kiddy, automatic scanning script, botnet script, whatever) with something thats such old news.

- David Gibbons


From alan at clueserver.org  Wed Feb 28 15:38:33 2007
From: alan at clueserver.org (alan)
Date: Wed, 28 Feb 2007 15:38:33 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] vulnerable Linux
In-Reply-To: <10922545.31172703897263.JavaMail.root@mail.dgibbons.net>
References: <10922545.31172703897263.JavaMail.root@mail.dgibbons.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0702281535250.20338@blackbox.fnordora.org>

On Wed, 28 Feb 2007, David A. Gibbons wrote:

>
> ----- "Michael Rasmussen" <mikeraz at patch.com> wrote:
>> In case you don't read LWN.
>>
>> Securing Linux by breaking it with Damn Vulnerable Linux
>>
>
>> DVL is a live CD available as a 150MB ISO. It's based on the popular
>> mini-Linux
>> distribution Damn Small Linux (DSL), not only for its minimal size,
>> but also for
>> the fact that DSL uses a 2.4 kernel, which makes it easier to offer
>> vulnerable
>> elements that might not work under the 2.6 kernel.
>
>
> That seems kinda stupid to me, I would imagine the amount of relevant knowledge to be gained by people breaking into worn aged software is much smaller than the insight that would be gained by a 2.6 kernel with lots of software not too old running on it.
>
> It's like saying there's tons to be gained by watching people hack windows 95. Your also much more likely to get a "false" positive (script kiddy, automatic scanning script, botnet script, whatever) with something thats such old news.

Actually there is a lot to be gained.  Understanding an exploit anh ow it 
works is not as easy as some people seem to believe.  Being able to write 
a heap exploit, getting it to work on a known piece of code, and then 
understanding why it worked is quite valuable.  You can then extend that 
knowledge to something that has not been rooted.

Not everything is a buffer overflow.

Learning from the mistakes of the past is a good thing.

"Invoking the supernatural can explain anything, and hence explains nothing."
                   - University of Utah bioengineering professor Gregory Clark

                                  will meet

                            Smith Memorial Center
                                   Room 294
                           On the block bounded by
       SW Montgomery, SW Broadway (7th), SW Harrison, and SW Park (9th)

      *******************************************************************

                                 PRESENTATION

                What went wrong with my disaster recovery plan

                                      by

                                 Brian Martin
                         <Brian at martinconsulting.com>


      Although many organizations have disaster recovery plans,
      an actual disaster can present a number of unexpected problems.
      From transportation difficulties to incorrect public information
      to clueless vendors, Brian Martin will share the realities he
      experienced during an actual disaster in the aftermath of the
      1989 Loma Prieta earthquake.
      This non-technical presentation will be suitable to anyone
      involved in or even thinking about disaster recovery.

      *******************************************************************

From johnxj at comcast.net  Wed Feb 28 20:10:12 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Wed, 28 Feb 2007 20:10:12 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Command line to get list of all hardware/drivers?
Message-ID: <20070228201012.d425eee7.johnxj@comcast.net>

Problem: There is something in this computer that is boogering up Edgy
amd64 so that certain features of OpenOffice.org are not working. Only
OOo is affected. I have tried the live/install CD for Edgy amd64 and
OOo still has exactly the same problems. I also tried the 32-bit Edgy
live/install CD and the problems went away. It has to be a hardware
conflict of some sort in 64-bit Edgy.

I have e-mail addresses for half a dozen other Edgy amd64 users who
have exactly the same set of problems. (We are rare.) I want to query
them and ask them to run a command that will generate a list of all
their hardware. I will also ask some Edgy amd64 users who do not have
the problem to do the same. Then I will compare the lists to see what
the commonalities are.

It doesn't have to be done at the command line, but I'm guessing that
would be simpler, faster and easier. I've looked at command line
documentation online for the past hour and have not found what I need.
But in Linux the command line is suppoed to be able to make you live
longer, be more attractive and get you more sex. Surely what I need is
a piece of cake. Can someone suggest something?

there's a debian package called `discover` that collects pci, usb, pcmcia device 
ID's and appropriate drivers (mostly kernel modules, but also x.org driver name).

should be available through apt I think. `discover` and `discover-data`

I found it in Synaptic, where the current version seems to be discover
1 and discover1-data. Both were already installed. I tried running it
from the command line but it didn't work:

jjj at Devil5:~$ discover
open_sock(): Operation not permitted
jjj at Devil5:~$ sudo discover
Password:
jjj at Devil5:~$ sudo discover1
sudo: discover1: command not found
jjj at Devil5:~$ sudo discover
jjj at Devil5:~$ 

I googled and discovered that it doesn't really "work." It is used to
detect hardware during bootup. So it doesn't create a report or
anything I could put into a text document.
